The Proof That Attribution Must Be Affirmational (Thubutiyya)
Proof that mawsufiyya must be thubutiyya by eliminating other possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a proof by elimination leads to the conclusion that the relation of attribution (mawṣūfiyya) cannot be existential or non-existential, and must be affirmational (thubūtiyya) for logical divisions to hold.

Core Idea
Attribution cannot be an existing thing or nothing—it must be a conceptual relation.

In this passage, the author proves that the relation between a subject and its attribute (mawṣūfiyya) is not an extra-existing entity, nor nothing at all. Through a rigorous elimination of possibilities, he demonstrates that the only coherent option is that this relation is a mental consideration (thubūtiyya). This argument underpins the logical basis for dividing things into existence and non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Notice the logical structure: an initial claim, then two major alternatives considered.
The passage uses a style of 'otherwise' clauses to eliminate possibilities.
2
Identify the initial division
The author begins by asserting that attribution must be thubutiyya, because if it were not, both contradictories (attribution and non-attribution) would be negated. This is the key starting point.
The 'otherwise' refers to the case where mawsufiyya is not thubutiyya.
3
Analyze the existential case
The author then considers if mawsufiyya were existential (wujudiyya). He examines three subcases: identical to both relata, a part of them, or external but subsisting in them. Recognize why each fails.
The first subcase is 'obviously false' because then the relata would not be conceivable without the relation.
4
Follow the regress argument
Focus on the external subcase: if mawsufiyya is a distinct entity subsisting in both, then it itself requires a relation to its relata, leading to an infinite regress. This is impossible.
The regress is avoided only if the relation is not a real entity.
5
Consider the final alternative
After eliminating existential and non-existential, the author concludes that if mawsufiyya is neither, then it cannot be a valid relation between subject and predicate. This undermines the logical division of things into existent and non-existent.
Thus, mawsufiyya must be thubutiyya (a mental consideration) to avoid all these problems.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالموصوفية ثبوتية وإلا ارتفع النقيضان ( أعني الموصوفية واللاموصوفية، إذ لا ثبوت لشيء منهما ولا وجودية (وإلا أي وإن كانت الموصوفية وجودية (فإما نفسهما ) أي نفس الموصوف والصفة فلا يعقلان (دونها وهي ظاهر البطلان، وكذا الحال إذا كانت الموصوفية جزءاً لهما ( أو غيرهما ) يعني به ما كان خارجاً عنهما قائماً بهما (فلهما) حينئذ (موصوفية بها أي بتلك الموصوفية القائمة بهما، فننقل الكلام إلى الموصوفية الثانية فإنها تكون أيضاً وجودية قائمة بطرفيها، فهناك موصوفية ثالثة فتتسلسل الموصوفيات إلى ما لا يتناهى وهو باطل، وإذا لم تكن الموصوفية عدمية ولا وجودية فلا يمكن اعتبارها بين الموضوع والمحمول اعتباراً صحيحاً، فلا يكون حينئذ للجزء الثبوتي من قولنا : الشيء إما أن يكون أو لا يكون
So attribution (al-mawṣūfiyya) is affirmational (thubūtiyya), otherwise the two contradictories (namely, attribution and non-attribution) would be lifted, since there is no affirmation (thubūt) for either of them. And it is not existential (wujūdiyya). Otherwise, if attribution is existential, then (it is) either the two themselves (i.e., the subject and the attribute themselves), so they are not conceivable without it, which is obviously false; similarly if attribution is a part of them, or something other than them (i.e., what is external to them and subsisting in them). Then for them there is an attribution by it (i.e., by that existential attribution subsisting in them). We then transfer the discussion to the second attribution, for it too would be existential subsisting in its two sides, so there would be a third attribution, and thus attributions regress infinitely, which is impossible. And if attribution is neither non-existential nor existential, then it cannot be validly considered between subject and predicate. Then there would be no affirmational part (juzʾ thubūtī) for our statement: 'A thing is either existent or non-existent.'
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a proof by division. The author argues that attribution (mawsufiyya) must be affirmational (thubutiyya) because if it were not, then both contradictories (attribution and non-attribution) would be negated, which is impossible. Then he considers the alternative: suppose attribution is existential (wujudiyya). In that case, it is either identical with both subject and attribute, in which case they are not conceivable without it (obviously false), or it is a part of them, or it is something else external to both but subsisting in them. In the last case, the attribution itself is an existential entity subsisting in the two relata, which then requires a second attribution to relate it to them, and that second attribution itself would be existential, leading to an infinite regress of attributions. Since all these options are impossible, the only remaining possibility is that attribution is neither non-existential nor existential, but then it cannot be validly posited between subject and predicate, meaning that the affirmative part (juzʾ thubutī) of the statement 'a thing is either existent or non-existent' would be unfounded. The conclusion is that the attribution relation cannot be coherently established, which undermines the basis of the logical division.

Key Terms
موصوفية
ثبوتية
وجودية
عدمية
تسلسل
نقيضان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof only shows that existential attribution leads to regress, but the alternative of non-existential attribution might still be viable.
Correction: The author shows that if attribution were non-existential ('adamiyya), then both contradictories (attribution and non-attribution) would be negated, which is impossible. Thus non-existential is also eliminated.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude if mawsufiyya is neither 'adamiyya nor wujudiyya?
A) It is thubutiyya.
B) It cannot be validly considered between subject and predicate.
C) It leads to infinite regress.
D) It is identical to the subject.

Key Takeaway
Attribution (mawṣūfiyya) is a conceptual relation (thubūtiyya), not an extra entity or nothing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If attribution is merely a conceptual relation, then your sin is not a 'thing' that adheres to your essence—yet it is truly attributed to you. When you repent, are you merely removing a label, or is there a real transformation in your relation to the attribute? How does this affect your urgency in seeking forgiveness?
?
This proof shows that the logical structure of reality depends on a foundation of conceptual relations that stop at a first (God's knowledge). Your own self-identification—are you attributing things to yourself that have no real basis? What is one false attribution you hold about yourself that you must now confront, knowing it cannot be a real existent?
?
The infinite regress argument demonstrates that every existential relation would require another, leading to impossibility. In your daily life, what attachments or desires are you pursuing that, if analyzed, would lead to an endless chain? How can you break that chain by affirming only the Ultimate Relation (God) as the foundation?

The Argument about al-Mawsufiyya and its Regress
Argument that al-mawsufiyya cannot be a real entity without regress, and its consequence for the principle of excluded middle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the relation of being qualified (al-mawsufiyya) cannot be a real entity without leading to an infinite regress, and how this challenges the certainty of the principle of excluded middle.

Core Idea
The relation of being qualified leads to infinite regress if taken as real.

This lesson presents a dense argument from classical kalam that targets a common logical principle. The argument shows that the relation of being qualified (al-mawsufiyya) between a subject and its attribute cannot be considered a real entity without causing an infinite regress. It then concludes that the positive part of the statement 'The thing either is or is not' is not a sound judgment. This challenges the status of the principle of excluded middle as a certain first principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Pay attention to the parentheses which indicate clarifications.
The passage is a single continuous argument; resist the urge to skip ahead.
2
Understand the initial dilemma
Identify the two initial possibilities: al-mawsufiyya is either positive (thubutiyya) or existential (wujudiyya). Notice the immediate consequence if it is not thubutiyya.
The text says 'otherwise the two contradictories would be removed'.
3
Examine the subcases of wujudiyya
If al-mawsufiyya is existential, it could be identical to the subject and attribute, or a part of them, or something external. Each possibility is examined and leads to a problem.
Focus on why the identical case is 'obviously false' and why the other cases lead to regress.
4
Recognize the infinite regress
When al-mawsufiyya is taken as external or a part, it requires another relation to connect it, leading to an infinite chain. This is declared false.
The term 'تتسلسل' indicates the regress to infinity.
5
Understand the conclusion
Since all options fail, al-mawsufiyya cannot be considered a real relation. Therefore, the positive part of the statement 'The thing either is or is not' has no valid meaning.
This conclusion undercuts the certainty of the principle of excluded middle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالموصوفية ثبوتية وإلا ارتفع النقيضان ( أعني الموصوفية واللاموصوفية، إذ لا ثبوت لشيء منهما ولا وجودية (وإلا أي وإن كانت الموصوفية وجودية (فإما نفسهما ) أي نفس الموصوف والصفة فلا يعقلان (دونها وهي ظاهر البطلان، وكذا الحال إذا كانت الموصوفية جزءاً لهما ( أو غيرهما ) يعني به ما كان خارجاً عنهما قائماً بهما (فلهما) حينئذ (موصوفية بها أي بتلك الموصوفية القائمة بهما، فننقل الكلام إلى الموصوفية الثانية فإنها تكون أيضاً وجودية قائمة بطرفيها، فهناك موصوفية ثالثة فتتسلسل الموصوفيات إلى ما لا يتناهى وهو باطل، وإذا لم تكن الموصوفية عدمية ولا وجودية فلا يمكن اعتبارها بين الموضوع والمحمول اعتباراً صحيحاً، فلا يكون حينئذ للجزء الثبوتي من قولنا : الشيء إما أن يكون أو لا يكون معنى صحيح
For al-mawsufiyya (the relation of being qualified) is positive (thubutiyya), otherwise the two contradictories (i.e., al-mawsufiyya and non-mawsufiyya) would be removed, since there is no affirmation for either of them and [it is] not existential. And if [it is] not, i.e., if al-mawsufiyya is existential, then either it is the same as the two [i.e., the subject and attribute themselves], so the subject and attribute cannot be conceived without it, which is obviously false; similarly if al-mawsufiyya is a part of them, or other than them, meaning something external to them subsisting in them, then they have at that time a mawsufiyya by it, i.e., by that existential mawsufiyya subsisting in them. Then we transfer the discussion to the second mawsufiyya, for it would also be existential subsisting in its two sides, so there is a third mawsufiyya, and thus the mawsufiyyāt regress infinitely, which is false. And if al-mawsufiyya is neither non-existential nor existential, then it is impossible to consider it between subject and predicate in a valid consideration; so then the positive part of our statement 'The thing either is or is not' has no valid meaning.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is an argument against taking the relation of being qualified (al-mawsufiyya) as a real entity. It begins by considering two possibilities: either this relation is positively existent (thubutiyya) or it is not. If it is not, then the two contradictories (mawsufiyya and its negation) would both be removed, which is impossible. If it is existent, then we examine its relation to the subject and attribute. If it is identical to them, then they cannot be conceived without it, which is false because we can conceive of a body without its blackness. If it is a part of them or something external, then it itself would need another mawsufiyya to relate it to them, leading to an infinite regress. The argument then concludes: since both options fail, the relation cannot be considered at all between subject and predicate, and therefore the statement 'Everything either is or is not' has no valid positive part (i.e., its positive part is not a genuine judgment). This argument aimed to show that the principle of excluded middle is not a certain first principle.

Key Terms
الموصوفية
ثبوتية
وجودية
عدم
التسلسل
الجزء الثبوتي
الموصوف
الصفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that there is any relation of being qualified at all.
Correction: The argument only denies that the relation can be a real entity; it does not deny the relation itself as a conceptual consideration.

Checkpoint
What is the argument's conclusion about the proposition 'The thing either is or is not'?
A) It is a certain first principle.
B) Its positive part has no valid meaning.
C) It is always false.
D) It must be considered as a negative judgment.

Key Takeaway
The relation of being qualified cannot be considered a real entity without leading to an infinite regress, thus the positive part of the principle of excluded middle is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that a seemingly basic logical relation, taken as real, leads to absurdity. Is there a belief you hold that you have never critically examined, yet it forms the foundation of your reasoning? What would it take for you to question that belief?
?
The passage concludes that the positive part of 'the thing either is or is not' has no valid meaning. Do you accept that? If so, how does it shake your confidence in everyday logical reasoning? If not, pinpoint the step in the argument where you resist, and ask yourself: why do I resist?
?
You rely on the principle of excluded middle constantly, often without thought. After reading this lesson, can you still assume that this principle is simply given? What does it mean for your intellectual humility if even the most basic logical tools are open to critique? Write down one assumption you hold about reality and mentally follow its logical consequences until you either see an infinite regress or reach a foundation. Then, decide what to do with that insight.

The Middle State and the Uncertainty of First Principles
Presenting the fourth proof using disagreement over a middle state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the disagreement over a middle state between existence and non-existence serves as a proof that even the most obvious first principles are not certain.

Core Idea
Disagreement among thinkers shows that even self-evident truths are not immune to doubt.

After arguing that the principle of excluded middle cannot be taken as a pure negation, our author presents a fourth powerful argument: the very existence of disagreement about a 'middle state' between existence and non-existence undermines the claim that the principle is self-evident. This argument does not require that the middle state actually exists—only that reasonable people have disagreed about it. By showing that error is possible even regarding such basic principles, the author aims to shake the certainty of all first principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins by introducing the 'fourth aspect' and then states the middle state.
2
Identify the key terms
Understand 'intermediate' (wāsita) and 'state' (ḥāl). These refer to a proposed third category between existence and non-existence. Note that the author says it will be explained later.
3
Analyze the disagreement
Two groups exist: a large minority affirms the middle state, the majority denies it and claims self-evidence. Realise that this disagreement itself is the crux of the proof.
The author does not say which side is correct; he only uses the fact of disagreement.
4
Follow the logic and conclusion
The argument follows a conditional chain: if the principle is self-evident, the first group erred; if not, the majority erred. In either case, error is possible, so no self-evident truth is certain. This is the desired conclusion.
The final sentence explicitly states that the proposition is not certain, and consequently nothing else is certain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معنى صحيح، فهو باطل قطعاً ( فإذا الحق ) منه هو ( السلب أبداً وأنتم لا تقولون به ) أي بتعين الحقية في الجزء السلبي . الوجه (الرابع) من الوجوه الأربعة الدالة على أن أجلى البديهيات ليس بيقيني أن يقال ( الواسطة) المسماة بالحال (ثابتة بينهما) أي بين الموجود والمعدوم ( لما سيأتي بيانه في الموقف الثاني ( وإذ أثبتها قوم بلغوا في الكثرة إلى حد تقوم الحجة بقولهم ونفاها الأكثرون وادعوا أن البديهة شاهدة بالانحصار في الموجود والمعدوم ) فأحد الفريقين اشتبه عليه البديهي وغيره ) فإن الانحصار فيهما إن كان بديهياً فقد اشتبه على الفرقة الأولى البديهي بغيره، وإلا فقد اشتبه على الأكثرين ما ليس بديهياً بالبديهي، وحيث جاز الاشتباه فيه ( فلا ثقة به ) بل ولا ثقة بشيء من البديهيات لجواز كونه من المشتبهات، فثبت بهذه الوجوه الأربعة أن قولنا الشيء إما أن يكون أو لا يكون ليس بيقيني، فلا يكون غيره أيضاً يقينياً وهو المطلوب
...a valid meaning, so it is absolutely false. So the truth is that it is always negation, and you do not say that. The fourth (aspect) of the four aspects indicating that the clearest of self-evident truths is not certain is that it is said: The intermediate (al-wāsita) called the state (al-ḥāl) is established between them, i.e., between the existent and the non-existent, as will be explained in the second station. And when some people established it, reaching in multitude to a degree that their statement constitutes a proof, and the majority denied it and claimed that intuition (al-badīha) witnesses the restriction to the existent and the non-existent. So one of the two groups mistook the self-evident for something else. For if the restriction to them is self-evident, then the first group mistook the self-evident for something else; otherwise, the majority mistook what is not self-evident for the self-evident. And since error is possible in it, there is no confidence in it. Rather, there is no confidence in any of the self-evident truths because of the possibility of its being among the ambiguous things. So it is established by these four aspects that our statement 'The thing either is or is not' is not certain, and therefore nothing else is certain either. That is what is sought.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument against the certainty of the principle of excluded middle. It begins by concluding from the previous argument that the proposition must always be taken as a negation, but the opponents do not accept that. Then it introduces the 'middle state' (al-wāsita) or 'state' (al-ḥāl) which some philosophers posit as intermediate between existence and non-existence. The argument notes that a large group of thinkers affirmed this middle state, while the majority denied it. The existence of such disagreement shows that the principle is not a matter of universal intuitive certainty. Either the principle is self-evident, in which case the minority who affirm a middle state have mistaken something else for the self-evident; or it is not self-evident, in which case the majority have mistaken what is not self-evident for the self-evident. In either case, the possibility of error means that we cannot be certain of any self-evident truth. Therefore, the proposition is not certain, and consequently no other first principle is certain either. This is the desired conclusion of the entire discussion.

Key Terms
الواسطة
الحال
الموجود
المعدوم
البديهة
اليقيني
الاشتباه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that the middle state actually exists.
Correction: The argument does not assert the reality of the middle state; it only uses the fact that some people have affirmed it to demonstrate that disagreement is possible, which undermines certainty.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the fourth proof?
A) The middle state is proven to exist.
B) The existence of disagreement shows the principle is not certain.
C) The majority always decides the truth.
D) Self-evident truths are always certain.

Key Takeaway
Disagreement over a middle state between existence and non-existence proves that even the most basic principle is not immune to error, undermining certainty in all self-evident truths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt absolutely certain about a belief, only to encounter someone equally intelligent who disagrees? How does this passage force you to reexamine the confidence you place in your own reasoning? Will you now seek evidence beyond your intuition?
?
If the most basic principle of logic—that something either exists or does not—can be doubted, what does that say about your reliance on your mind in matters of creed? Are you willing to admit that your certainty in any doctrine might be a product of mistaken intuition?
?
This passage uses disagreement itself as proof of possible error. Think of a theological disagreement you have witnessed: does this argument challenge your impulse to dismiss the other side as simply ignorant? What would it cost you to hold your beliefs with humility rather than certainty?

Distinguishing the Fourth and Second Proofs
Al-Jurjānī distinguishes the fourth proof from the second and corrects a grammatical error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fourth proof and the second proof differ in what they establish: the fourth disproves the validity of the proposition, while the second disproves its certainty.

Core Idea
The fourth proof says the proposition is invalid; the second says it lacks certainty.

In his commentary, al-Jurjānī clarifies that the fourth proof and the second proof serve different logical roles. The fourth proof directly attacks the validity of the statement that every thing must either be or not be, while the second proof questions whether we can be certain of it. Understanding this distinction is essential for following the rest of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two proofs and their claims
Find where al-Jurjānī states what the fourth proof establishes and what the second proof establishes. Notice the different outcomes: 'invalidity' vs 'lack of certainty'.
3
Note the correction about coordination
Al-Jurjānī corrects a potential misunderstanding about how the second proof is grammatically linked. This affects the logical structure.
The correction shows careful attention to the original author's wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( الواسطة ثابتة إلخ هذا الوجه يفيد عدم صحة قولنا : الشيء إما أن يكون أو لا يكون، والوجه الثاني أعني قوله : وإذ أثبتها يفيد عدم قطعيته فهو معطوف على قوله الواسطة ثابتة، وعطفه على قوله : لما سيأتي وهم . قوله : ( إلى حد تقوم الحجة إلخ أي في بعض المواد وهو ما إذا أخبروا عن المحسوس، وفائدة اعتبار الكثرة إلى هذا الحد الإشارة إلى أن الكثرة الزائدة في جانب نفي الواسطة لا ترفع الاشتباه، لأن كلا الفريقين تقوم بقولهم الحجة في المحسوسات واحتمال تطرق الغلط في المعقولات جار فيهما . قوله : (بل ولا ثقة إلخ لا يخفى أن هذا الإضراب مستدرك إذ يكفي قوله : فثبت بهذه الوجوه إلخ في إتمام الوجوه الأربعة . قوله : ( وستعرف جواب إلخ إما إشارة إلى ما ذكره في مبحث الحال، من أن عدم الواسطة بين النفي والإثبات ،ضروري والواسطة إنما تثبت إذا فسر الموجود بمعنى الموجود أصالة، قوله : ( الواسطة ثابتة بينهما إلخ لا يذهب عليك أن الحكم بثبوت الواسطة والاستدلال عليه بدليلين كما هو الظاهر لغو، إذ يكفي أن يقول : أثبت القوم الواسطة ونفاها الأكثرون . قوله : ( وإذ أثبتها قوم بلغوا إلخ ظاهره أنه معطوف على قوله : لما سيأتي، فإذا هو دليل آخر على ثبوت الواسطة فلذا رد عليه بأن كثرة القائلين في العقليات لا تكون حجة قال في شرح المقاصد وما ذكر في المواقف من أن القائلين بها بلغوا في الكثرة حداً تقوم الحجة بقولهم : معناه أنه قد يكون حجة، وذلك عند الإخبار عن المحسوس ففي المعقول يكون شبهة لا أقل. قوله : (بل ولا ثقة إلخ) والظاهر مما ذكر عدم الوثوق بهذا البديهي المخصوص، فلهذا لم يرجع ضمير به إلى مطلق البديهي واحتاج إلى ذلك الترقي .
His statement: 'The middle is established, etc.' This aspect indicates the invalidity of our statement 'The thing either is or is not.' The second aspect, namely his statement: 'And when some people established it', indicates its lack of certainty; so it is coordinated with his statement 'the middle is established', and coordinating it with his statement 'as will be explained' is a mistake. His statement: 'to the degree that their statement constitutes a proof etc.' means in some matters, namely when they report about the sensible. The benefit of considering multitude to this degree is to indicate that the excess multitude on the side of denying the middle does not remove the error, because each of the two groups' statements constitute a proof concerning sensibles, and the possibility of error in intelligibles applies to both. His statement: 'Rather, there is no confidence etc.' It is not hidden that this transition (iḍrāb) is unnecessary, for his statement 'So it is established by these aspects etc.' suffices to complete the four aspects. His statement: 'And you will know the answer etc.' is either a reference to what he mentioned in the discussion of the state (al-ḥāl), that the absence of a middle between negation and affirmation is necessary, and the middle is only established if 'existent' is interpreted as meaning primarily existent. His statement: 'The middle is established between them etc.' Let it not escape you that the judgment of the establishment of the middle and the proof of it by two proofs, as is apparent, is superfluous, for it suffices to say: The people established the middle and the majority denied it. His statement: 'And when some people established it, reaching etc.' Its apparent meaning is that it is coordinated with his statement 'as will be explained', so it is another proof for the establishment of the middle. Hence it is replied to that the multitude of proponents in intellectual matters is not a proof. He said in Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid: 'What is mentioned in al-Mawāqif that the proponents reached a multitude to the degree that their statement constitutes a proof means that it may be a proof, and that is when reporting about the sensible; as for the intellectual, it is a mere doubt at least.' His statement: 'Rather, there is no confidence etc.' The apparent meaning from what was mentioned is the lack of confidence in this particular self-evident truth, and for this reason the pronoun 'it' did not refer to absolute self-evident truth, and this escalation (taraqqī) was needed.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary by the author (al-Jurjānī) on the fourth proof and related points. It begins by distinguishing the fourth proof from the second: the fourth proof shows that the proposition is not valid (i.e., false), while the second proof shows it is not certain. The author corrects a misunderstanding about the coordination of clauses. Then he explains that the multitude of proponents is only significant in matters of sense perception, not in intellectual matters; thus the disagreement does not prove the existence of the middle state but only shows the possibility of error. He also criticizes the wording of the original text, calling some parts superfluous. He notes that the response to the fourth proof is deferred and hints at the solution that the middle state is only possible if 'existent' is interpreted as 'existent in itself' (mawjūd aṣāla). Finally, he explains the need for the escalation in the last phrase, showing that the pronoun refers specifically to this particular self-evident truth, not to self-evident truths in general. This commentary clarifies the logical structure of the four proofs and their intended conclusions.

Key Terms
الوجه الرابع
الوجه الثاني
القطع
الصحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the fourth proof and the second proof aim to disprove the same thing.
Correction: In fact, the fourth proof disproves validity, while the second proof disproves certainty—two distinct failures.

Checkpoint
What does the second proof establish according to the commentary?
A) The proposition is false.
B) The proposition is not certain.
C) The proposition is self-evident.
D) The proposition is true.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof invalidates the proposition, while the second proof shows it is not certain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson corrects a subtle grammatical error that could lead to misunderstanding. Is there a point in your faith or reasoning that you have assumed correctly without checking the details? What will you do today to verify one such assumption?
?

Multitude of Proponents: Sensibles vs. Intelligibles and the Middle State
Al-Jurjānī explains the limitation of multitude as proof and hints at the solution for the middle state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the multitude of proponents is only a valid proof in matters of sense perception, not in intellectual matters, and how the middle state is only possible under a specific interpretation of 'existent'.

Core Idea
Multitude proves nothing in intellectual matters; only sense reports can be confirmed by consensus.

Al-Jurjānī explains that when a group of people attests to a sensible fact, their consensus can be considered proof, but in abstract intellectual questions, even a large number of proponents does not settle the issue. He also points to a later discussion where the middle state is shown to be necessary only if 'existent' is taken as 'existent in itself'. This lesson explores those distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on multitude and proof
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall meaning. Focus on the distinction between sense perception (mahsūs) and intellectual matters (maʿqūlāt).
2
Understand the role of multitude in sensibles vs. intelligibles
Al-Jurjānī states that multitude matters only in sense perception. In intellectual matters, error is possible for both sides. This limits the force of the argument from consensus.
3
Note the critique of the wording
The commentary criticizes the original text for making the transition (iḍrāb) unnecessary. This shows attention to rhetorical precision.
4
Preview the deferred answer about the middle state
The passage refers to a later discussion where the solution is given: the middle state is necessary only if 'existent' means 'existent in itself'.
This connects to the broader debate about the nature of existence.

The Fourth Argument: Doubt from a Disputed Middle State
Al-Jurjani presents the fourth argument challenging self-evident certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the disagreement over a purported middle state between existence and non-existence is used to challenge the certainty of the most self-evident proposition.

Core Idea
Disagreement among scholars can undermine even what seems self-evident.

Al-Jurjani presents the fourth and final argument in a series aimed at showing that even the clearest self-evident truths are not certain. This argument zeroes in on a disputed concept: an intermediary state called al-ḥāl. The very fact that scholars disagree over its existence introduces doubt into the principle of excluded middle, and thereby into all self-evident knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target proposition
Read the passage and note that the proposition under attack is 'a thing is either existent or non-existent' – the law of excluded middle.
This is considered the most obvious self-evident truth.
2
Understand the intermediary (al-wāsiṭa)
The argument posits an intermediary state called al-ḥāl between existence and non-existence. Some affirm it, most deny it. Recognize that this disagreement is the key to the argument.
The existence of al-ḥāl is debated in kalām; it refers to states that are neither fully existent nor non-existent.
3
Trace the confusion claim
If the intermediary is real, then the majority who deny it have confused the self-evident; if it's not real, then the proponents have confused it. Either way, confusion about the self-evident is possible.
This shows that self-evidence does not guarantee immunity from error.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since confusion is possible in this case, no self-evident proposition can be trusted absolutely. Hence, no proposition is certain. Understand that this is the desired conclusion for the skeptic.
The argument's goal is to undermine certainty in all knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع من الوجوه الأربعة الدالة على أن أجلى البديهيات ليس بيقيني أن يقال الواسطة المسماة بالحال ثابتة بينهما أي بين الموجود والمعدوم لما سيأتي بيانه في الموقف الثاني وإذ أثبتها قوم بلغوا في الكثرة إلى حد تقوم الحجة بقولهم ونفاها الأكثرون وادعوا أن البديهة شاهدة بالانحصار في الموجود والمعدوم فأحد الفريقين اشتبه عليه البديهي وغيره فإن الانحصار فيهما إن كان بديهياً فقد اشتبه على الفرقة الأولى البديهي بغيره وإلا فقد اشتبه على الأكثرين ما ليس بديهياً بالبديهي وحيث جاز الاشتباه فيه فلا ثقة به بل ولا ثقة بشيء من البديهيات لجواز كونه من المشتبهات فثبت بهذه الوجوه الأربعة أن قولنا الشيء إما أن يكون أو لا يكون ليس بيقيني فلا يكون غيره أيضاً يقينياً وهو المطلوب.
The fourth of the four indications that the clearest of self-evident propositions is not certain is that it is said that the intermediary called the state (al-ḥāl) is established between them, i.e., between the existent and the non-existent, due to what will be explained in the Second Station; and since a group who reached in multitude to the extent that proof is established by their statement affirmed it, while the majority denied it and claimed that intuition witnesses the restriction to the existent and the non-existent. So one of the two parties confused the self-evident with something else: for if the restriction to them is self-evident, then the first party confused the self-evident with something else; and if not, then the majority confused what is not self-evident with the self-evident. And since confusion is possible concerning it, there is no trust in it—rather, there is no trust in any of the self-evident propositions due to the possibility that it is among the confused things. So by these four indications it is established that our statement 'a thing is either existent or not existent' is not certain, so nothing else is certain either, and that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument (al-wajh al-rābiʿ) from a set of four that aim to show that even the most obvious self-evident propositions (ajlā al-badīhiyyāt) are not certain (yaqīnī). The argument centers on the possibility of a middle state (al-wāsiṭa) between existence and non-existence, called the state (al-ḥāl). The author notes that some thinkers affirm such a state, while the majority deny it, each claiming that self-evidence supports their view. This disagreement shows that one of the parties has mistaken the self-evident for something else, thus introducing doubt into the self-evident proposition 'a thing is either existent or not existent.' If confusion is possible here, then no self-evident truth can be trusted absolutely. Therefore, the original proposition is not certain, and by extension, no other proposition is certain either. The argument concludes that the desired point (al-maṭlūb)—the lack of certainty in self-evident propositions—is established.

Key Terms
الواسطة
الحال
البديهي
اليقيني
الموجود
المعدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-evident propositions are always absolutely certain and immune to doubt.
Correction: This passage shows that even the most self-evident proposition can be subject to disagreement, introducing the possibility of confusion and thus undermining absolute certainty.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth argument use to challenge the certainty of the self-evident proposition 'a thing is either existent or non-existent'?
A) The impossibility of a middle state
B) The disagreement over a middle state called al-ḥāl
C) The logical contradiction in the concept of non-existence
D) The majority opinion that the proposition is certain

Key Takeaway
The possibility of confusion about the most obvious self-evident truth means that no self-evident proposition can be considered absolutely certain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been absolutely certain about a belief in your faith, only to encounter a scholarly disagreement that shook that certainty? How did you respond, and what does this lesson teach you about handling such doubts?
?
This argument suggests that because there is disagreement about the most self-evident truth, we cannot trust any self-evident proposition. Does this challenge your confidence in the basic principles of your religion? How will you reconcile this skepticism with the need for a firm foundation in faith?
?
If the law of excluded middle can be contested, what does that mean for your own reasoning? Are you willing to examine your most cherished assumptions, even those you consider self-evident, and subject them to scrutiny as this lesson does?

Responding to Objections on the Concept of Non-Existent
Response to two objections on the concept of non-existent by distinguishing essence and concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends the concept of non-existent (ma'dum) against objections by distinguishing between its essence and its concept, and why this distinction resolves the apparent paradox.

Core Idea
The concept of non-existent is real in the mind, but its essence has no external reality.

This passage addresses a critical objection in kalam: how can the clearest axioms depend on conceiving something non-existent? The commentator skillfully distinguishes between the essence of the non-existent (which cannot be conceived) and its concept (which is mentally fixed). By granting only the latter, he avoids any contradiction while preserving the foundation of rational knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections
Read the passage to find the two objections: (1) that axioms depend on conceiving the non-existent, and (2) that axioms require distinguishing the non-existent from the existent. Notice how the commentator responds to each.
The key distinction is between essence (dhat) and concept (mafhum).
2
Understand the main distinction
Focus on the commentator’s move: if you mean ‘essence’ of the non-existent, the dependence is denied; if you mean ‘concept’, it is granted. This resolves the problem without granting reality to the non-existent.
The concept is fixed in the mind and distinct, but the absolute non-existent has no individual.
3
Trace the part/whole argument
Examine the passage where it says non-existence is not a part of the absolute non-existent. Understand that the counterpart for non-existent is negation of non-existence, not of the non-existent.
This prevents the absurdity of making the non-existent a part of something.
4
Consider the hypothetical resolution
Note the final part where the commentator brings up the philosophers’ view and the mutakallimun’s alternative. The answer is framed as hypothetical (فَرضي) to avoid absurdity.
This shows the sophistication of kalam in handling difficult logical issues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متصوراً ولكونه محكوماً عليه بالانفصال بينه وبين الموجود (و) هو (الثابت) لكونه متميزاً وهذا الذي ذكره جواب عن الوجهين الأولين وتوضيحه أن يقال : إن أردتم بما ذكرتم في الوجه الأول من أن أجلي البديهيات يتوقف على تصور المعدوم أنه يتوقف على تصور ذات المعدوم، فهو ممنوع وإن أردتم به توقفه على تصور مفهوم المعدوم فهو مسلم ويلزم حينئذ أن يكون مفهوم المعدوم متميزاً وثابتاً في الذهن، ولا استحالة فيه إنما المستحيل أن يكون ما صدق عليه مفهوم المعدوم المطلق ثابتا بوجه، وإن أردتم بما ذكرتم في الوجه الثاني من أن أجلى البديهيات يقتضي تميز المعدوم عن الموجود أنه يقتضي تميز ذات المعدوم المطلق، حتى يلزم أن يكون ذاته ثابتاً ما منعناه، وإن أردتم به أنه يقتضي تميز مفهوم المعدوم المطلق كما هو الظاهر من عبارتكم سلمناه، فيكون لمفهومه حقيقة وللعقل سلبها، فهناك عدم خاص قد عرض لمفهوم المعدوم مطلقاً، وليس في ذلك كون قسم من الشيء قسيماً له، وإنما يلزم هذا قوله : ( فهو ممنوع) لأن الذات لم يقع محمولاً . بوجه قوله : ( ولا استحالة فيه إذ اللازم منه أن يكون الشيء متصفاً بنقيضه، وذلك متحقق فإن مفهوم اللامعلوم معلوم والوجود معدوم ، إنما المحال أن يصدق النقيضان على شيء واحد، وليس للمعدوم المطلق فرد في نفس الأمر حتى يلزم من صدق مفهوم المعدوم عليه في نفس الأمر ثبوته فيه بناء على اتصافه بمفهوم ثبوتي فيلزم اجتماع النقيضين قوله : (كون قسم من الشيء قسيماً له إذ القسم للمعدوم المطلق سلب العدم لا سلب المعدوم، وقيل: لأن العدم ليس قسماً من المعدوم المطلق المراد به المعدوم في الذهن، والخارج إذ العدم موجود في الذهن والخارج، ولأن العدم ليس بمعدوم وإلا لزم ثبوت الشيء لنفسه كما مفهوم المعدوم أن في نفس الأمر ذاتاً ثبت له هذا المفهوم العدمي، أو ثبت له انتفاء مفهوم الوجود عنه فتأمل . قوله : ( وهو الثابت لكونه متميزاً هذا إنما يلزم مذهب الفلاسفة وأما الجواب عند المتكلمين النافين للوجود الذهني فهو منع اقتضاء التصور والتميز الثبوت . قوله : ( ولا استحالة فيه إلخ ) فيه بحث لأن مفهوم المعدوم المطلق إذا لزم تميزه وثبوته في نفسه، ولا شك في ثبوته لذاته عاد المحذور المذكور، وهو ثبوت المعدوم المطلق لأن ثبوته إنما كان لزم من اتصافه بأمر ثبوتي هو التميز وهو لزوم اتصافه بأمر ثبوتي آخر، وكذا الكلام إذا جعل جوابا عن الوجه الثاني، والجواب أن اتصاف ذات المعدوم المطلق بمفهومه على تقدير أن يتصور شيء منهما، وأن يكون مفهوم المعدوم المطلق مسلوباً عنه الوجود المطلق، وحينئذ لا محذور إذ هو فرضي كما قيل مثله في مسألة المجهول المطلق فلا محذور فتأمل.
Conceived as it is judged upon the separation between it and the existent, and it is the established because it is distinguished. This which he mentioned is an answer to the first two aspects, and its clarification is that it is said: If you mean by what you mentioned in the first aspect that the clearest of axioms depends on conceiving the non-existent—that it depends on conceiving the essence of the non-existent—then it is denied; and if you mean by it its dependence on conceiving the concept of the non-existent, then it is granted, and it follows then that the concept of the non-existent is distinguished and established in the mind, and there is no impossibility in it—the impossible is only that what is true of the concept of the absolute non-existent be established in any way. And if you mean by what you mentioned in the second aspect that the clearest of axioms necessitates the distinction of the non-existent from the existent—that it necessitates the distinction of the essence of the absolute non-existent such that its essence would be established—we deny that; and if you mean by it that it necessitates the distinction of the concept of the absolute non-existent, as is apparent from your expression, we grant it. So its concept has a reality, and the intellect has its negation. There is a particular non-existence that has occurred to the concept of the non-existent absolutely, and there is not in that a part of a thing being a counterpart to it; and this only follows from his saying: (so it is denied) because the essence is not predicated in any way. His saying: (and there is no impossibility in it) because what follows from it is that a thing be qualified by its opposite, and that is realized—for the concept of the not-known is known, and existence is non-existent—the impossible is only that two opposites be true of one thing, and the absolute non-existent has no individual in the external world such that from the truth of the concept of the non-existent upon it in the external world, its subsistence in it would follow, based on its being qualified by a positive concept, thus the conjunction of opposites would follow. His saying: (a part of a thing being a counterpart to it) because the part for the absolute non-existent is the negation of non-existence, not the negation of the non-existent; and it was said: because non-existence is not a part of the absolute non-existent meant by non-existent in the mind and external, for non-existence exists in the mind and external, and because non-existence is not non-existent, otherwise the subsistence of a thing for itself would follow. The concept of the non-existent is that in the external world an essence subsists for which this non-existent concept is established, or for which the negation of the concept of existence is established. So reflect. His saying: (and it is the established because it is distinguished) this follows only from the philosophers' doctrine; but the answer according to the mutakallimun who deny mental existence is to deny that conception and distinction necessitate subsistence. His saying: (and there is no impossibility in it, etc.) contains a discussion, because if the concept of the absolute non-existent requires its distinction and subsistence in itself—and there is no doubt about its subsistence for itself—the mentioned objection returns, namely the subsistence of the absolute non-existent, because its subsistence was only necessitated by its being qualified by a positive thing which is distinction, and that is its being qualified by another positive thing. And similarly the speech if it is made an answer to the second aspect. And the answer is that the qualification of the essence of the absolute non-existent by its concept, on the supposition that something of them is conceived, and that the concept of the absolute non-existent is negated of absolute existence; then there is no objection, for it is hypothetical, as a similar case was said in the issue of the absolutely unknown; so there is no objection. So reflect.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument regarding the concept of the non-existent (ma'dum) in relation to the clearest axioms (ajla al-badihiyyat). The commentator responds to two objections that claim that the clearest axioms depend on conceiving the non-existent or require its distinction from the existent. The key move is distinguishing between the essence (dhat) of the non-existent and its concept (mafhum). The first objection: if the dependence is on the essence, it is denied because the essence of the non-existent is not something that can be conceived as a subject. If it is on the concept, it is granted, but this implies only that the concept is distinct and fixed in the mind, which is not impossible. The impossible would be if the absolute non-existent itself had a fixed reality. The second objection: the distinction required is between the essence of the non-existent and the existent—this is denied; rather, the distinction is between concepts. The concept of the non-existent is a real mental entity, and its negation is in the intellect. The commentator then addresses a further concern: that this would make the non-existent a part of something and its counterpart. He explains that the non-existent (ma'dum) is not a part; rather, the part for absolute non-existent is the negation of non-existence (salb al-'adam), not the negation of the non-existent. He also discusses the possibility of a non-existent concept applying to something in the external world, concluding that the absolute non-existent has no individual, so no real problem arises. The answer given in the text is framed as a hypothetical (فرضي) to avoid absurdity.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the clearest axioms cannot depend on the concept of non-existent because that would grant reality to non-existence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only the concept is fixed in the mind, not the essence. The absolute non-existent has no individual, so no impossibility arises.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction that resolves the objection about the clearest axioms depending on the non-existent?
A) The distinction between essence and concept
B) The distinction between mental and external existence
C) The distinction between necessary and contingent
D) The distinction between subject and predicate

Key Takeaway
The concept of non-existent is a valid mental entity; its fixedness in the mind does not imply any external reality for the non-existent itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about death—your own non-existence—are you aware that the concept of 'you not existing' is a mental reality? Does that awareness change how you fear or prepare for it? What action can you take today to align your life with the truth that your mental concepts do not always match external reality?
?
Can you identify a belief you hold that you have mistakenly assumed corresponds to something real outside your mind, when in fact it is only a concept? How would distinguishing essence from concept help you correct that belief? Commit to examining one such belief this week.
?
The passage shows that even the clearest axioms require careful distinction. In your own pursuit of certainty, do you ever confuse ease of mental conception with truth? What is one area of your faith where you have settled for a comfortable concept rather than confronting the reality? What step can you take to move from concept to reality?

The Dual Nature of Non-Existence of Absolute Non-Existence
Commentator resolves part/counterpart dual nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the non-existence of absolute non-existence can be both a part of absolute non-existence and a counterpart to it, depending on the perspective.

Core Idea
One thing can be both part and counterpart if considered differently.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a potential contradiction: how can the non-existence of absolute non-existence be both a part (qism) and a counterpart (qasim) of absolute non-existence? The resolution lies in the two different considerations: as a restricted non-existence, it is a part; as a removal of non-existence, it is a counterpart. This clarification is essential for understanding the logical relationships between concepts in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the key terms: qism (part) and qasim (counterpart).
Notice the phrase 'min haythu annahu raf‘' and 'min haythu annahu ‘adam khāṣṣ'.
2
Understand the two considerations
The commentator explains that the same thing can be viewed in two ways: as a specific type of non-existence (part) and as a removal of absolute non-existence (counterpart).
This resolves the apparent contradiction.
3
Reflect on the logical principle
Ask yourself: why is this distinction important for kalam? It shows that non-existence can be analyzed without contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ( وليس في ذلك كون قسم من الشيء قسيماً له لأن العدم الخاص ليس قسيماً من في رفع حقيقة العدم، ولا استحالة فيه أيضاً، إذ يكون عدم العدم المطلق من حيث أنه رفع للعدم المطلق قسيماً له، ومن حيث أنه عدم خاص قسماً منه، ( والحمل) أي حمل الموجود على السواد إنما صح ( للتغاير مفهوماً فإن مفهوم السواد مغاير لمفهوم الموجود والاتحاد (هوية) أي ذاتاً صدقاً عليه فلا يلزم هاهنا عدم الإفادة، كما في قولنا : أنه ليس بموجود أيضاً، ولا يلزم ثبوت الواسطة لأن العدم لا يقبل هذه القسمة، وليس بشيء أما أولاً فلان العبارة لا تساعده، إذ اللائق حينئذ وليس في ذلك كون قسيم الشيء قسماً منه، وأما ثانياً فلان الكلام في عدم العدم المطلق، وأنه قسم من العدم المطلق، وقسيم له فالقول : بأنه ليس قسماً من المعدوم المطلق لا دخل له فيما نحن فيه، وأما ثالثاً فلان القول : بأن العدم موجود في الذهن مما لا معنی له، لأن الأعدام كلها من جملة المعدومات، كما صرح به الشارح في بحث تمايز المعدومات نعم إنه بعد التصور موجود في الذهن والكلام هاهنا في نفس العدم، وأما رابعاً فلان القول : بأن العدم ليس بمعدوم ولا موجود إنما هو في العدم المطلق، والكلام هاهنا في عدم العدم المطلق وهو عدم خاص. قوله : (إذ يكون عدم العدم المطلق إلخ) يعني أن هذا المقيد من حيث إنه عدم مقيد مع قطع النظر عن خصوصية القيد نوع منه ومن حيث إنه رفع للعدم مقابل له، فالمنظور في الاعتبار الأول كونه عدماً مقيداً بقيد وحينئذ الاعتبار الثاني هو كونه رفع العدم وسلبه، فالموضع مختلف باعتبار كذا أفاده بعض المحققين. ، قوله ( والاتحاد هوبة) قال المصنف في بحث الماهية ومعنى حمل الحيوان على الإنسان هذين المفهومين المتغايرين في العقل هويتهما الخارجية والوهمية واحدة، فلا يلزم وحدة الاثنين ولا حمل الشيء على نفسه، وقال الشارح: إن التفسير المذكور لا يطرد في نحو الإنسان أعمى، إذ لا هوية لمفهوم الأعمى متحدة مع هوية الإنسان وإلا لكان موجوداً خارجياً، فلهذا صرف المتن عن ظاهره وفسره بما هو المختار عنده أي الاتحاد هوية باعتبار الصدق لا أن المعدوم المطلق المراد به المعدوم في الذهن والخارج، إذ العدم موجود في الذهن ولأن العدم ليس بمعدوم، وإلا لزم ثبوت الشيء لنفسه كما أنه ليس بموجود أيضاً، ولا يلزم ثبوت الواسطة لأن العدم لا يقبل هذه القسمة كما أشير إليه في التجريد معين قوله : ( من حيث إنه رفع للعدم المطلق أراد بالعدم المطلق العدم الغير المضاف إلى شيء لا العدم في الذهن والخارج أي عدم الوجود الذهني والخارجي، كما أن المراد بالعدم المطلق المعدوم فيهما وإلا لم يصح كون العدم الخاص قسما منه، إذ لا يصدق عليه أنه عدم الوجود المطلق، بل هو عدم العدم فإن قلت: قسم الشيء مثبت له لا رافع، وأيضاً رفع العدم وجود وهو لا يكون قسماً من العدم بالبداهة قلت القسمية الإثبات بحسب الذات، والقسيمية الرفع بحسب المفهوم، ثم رفع العدم مستلزم للوجود لا نفسه وإن أشعر به كلام الشارح في بحث التقابل والاستلزام لا يقدح في القسمية .
His saying: (and there is not in that a part of a thing being a counterpart to it) because the particular non-existence is not a counterpart of in the removal of the reality of non-existence, and there is no impossibility in it also, since the non-existence of absolute non-existence, inasmuch as it is a removal of absolute non-existence, is a counterpart to it, and inasmuch as it is a particular non-existence, is a part of it. And (the predication) i.e., predicating existence on blackness, is only correct (due to difference in concept) for the concept of blackness is different from the concept of the existent, and the union (in quiddity) i.e., essentially in terms of truth, so there is no lack of benefit here, as in our saying: it is not existent also. And the subsistence of a middle does not follow because non-existence does not admit this division. And it is not a thing: firstly because the expression does not support it, for it would be fitting then that it is not in that a counterpart of a thing being a part of it; secondly because the discussion is about the non-existence of absolute non-existence, and that it is a part of absolute non-existence and a counterpart to it, so the saying that it is not a part of the absolute non-existent has no bearing on what we are discussing; thirdly because the saying that non-existence exists in the mind has no meaning, for non-existences are all among non-existents, as the commentator stated in the discussion of the differentiation of non-existents—yes, after conception it exists in the mind, but the discussion here is about non-existence itself; fourthly because the saying that non-existence is neither non-existent nor existent is only about absolute non-existence, and the discussion here is about the non-existence of absolute non-existence, which is a particular non-existence. His saying: (since the non-existence of absolute non-existence, etc.) means that this restricted thing, inasmuch as it is a non-existence restricted by a restriction, without consideration of the particularity of the restriction, is a type of it, and inasmuch as it is a removal of non-existence, is opposite to it. So the standpoint in the first consideration is that it is a non-existence restricted by a restriction, and then the second consideration is that it is a removal and negation of non-existence. So the position differs according to consideration—thus said some investigators. His saying: (and the union in quiddity). The author said in the discussion of quiddity: the meaning of predicating 'animal' on 'human' is that these two concepts that are different in the mind, their external and imaginary quiddity is one. So neither the unity of two nor the predication of a thing on itself follows. And the commentator said: The mentioned interpretation does not apply in cases like 'human is blind', for there is no quiddity of the concept of 'blind' united with the quiddity of 'human', otherwise it would be an external existent. Therefore, he diverted the text from its apparent meaning and interpreted it according to his chosen view, i.e., the union in quiddity in terms of truth, not that the absolute non-existent is meant by the non-existent in the mind and the external, for non-existence exists in the mind, and because non-existence is not non-existent, otherwise the subsistence of a thing for itself would follow, as it is also not existent. And the subsistence of a middle does not follow, because non-existence does not admit this division, as indicated in the Tajrid. A specific saying: (inasmuch as it is a removal of absolute non-existence). He meant by absolute non-existence the non-existence not attached to anything, not non-existence in the mind and external, i.e., non-existence of mental and external existence. Just as the absolute non-existent is meant by the non-existent in both. Otherwise, it would not be correct for the particular non-existence to be a part of it, for it would not be true of it that it is non-existence of absolute existence; rather, it is non-existence of non-existence. If you say: a part of a thing is affirmative of it, not removal; also the removal of non-existence is existence, and it is not a part of non-existence by intuition. I say: the partiality is affirmation in terms of essence, and the counterpart-hood is removal in terms of concept. Then the removal of non-existence entails existence, not existence itself, even if the commentator's speech in the discussion of opposition and entailment suggests it, it does not harm the partiality.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a clarification of a previous gloss regarding the non-existence of absolute non-existence (adam al-'adam al-mutlaq). The commentator explains that the same thing can be considered from two perspectives: as a part of a genus (qism) when seen as a restricted non-existence, and as an opposite (qasim) when seen as a removal of that genus. This resolves the concern that something cannot be both a part and a counterpart. Then the passage turns to the issue of predication (haml). It explains that predicating existence on blackness is only correct if there is a difference in concept (taghayur mafhuman) but unity in quiddity (ittihad hawiyyatan) in terms of truth. This avoids tautology. The commentator then addresses four objections about non-existence: (1) The expression about 'not being a part' is clarified; (2) The discussion is specifically about adam al-'adam al-mutlaq, not about absolute non-existent; (3) Non-existence as such does not exist in the mind, but its concept does after conception; (4) The statement that non-existence is neither existent nor non-existent applies only to absolute non-existence, not to the particular non-existence under discussion. Finally, the commentary on 'ittihad hawiyyatan' explains that in predications like 'human is blind', there is no real unity of quiddity, so the predication is based on truth (sidq) rather than identity. The passage concludes with a subtle point about the removal of non-existence entailing existence while not being existence itself, preserving the logical distinction.

Key Terms
عدم العدم المطلق
قسم
قسيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A single entity cannot be both a part and a counterpart of the same thing.
Correction: It can be when considered from different perspectives: as a specific type (part) and as a removal of the whole (counterpart).

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why can the non-existence of absolute non-existence be both a part and a counterpart?
A) It is a part when considered as a removal and a counterpart when considered as a particular non-existence.
B) It is a part when considered as a particular non-existence and a counterpart when considered as a removal.
C) It is a part in the mind and a counterpart in reality.
D) It is a part for some things and a counterpart for others.

Key Takeaway
The non-existence of absolute non-existence is both a part (as a specific type) and a counterpart (as a removal) depending on the perspective.

?

Predication Requires Difference in Concept and Unity in Quiddity
Commentary on predication condition and objections about non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why predicating existence on blackness requires difference in concept but unity in quiddity, and how this rule applies to statements about non-existence.

Core Idea
Predication needs conceptual difference but real unity.

The commentator now turns to the topic of predication (haml). He explains that when we say 'blackness is existent', the predication is valid only because the concept of blackness is different from the concept of existence, yet they are united in reality (the blackness itself is existent). This avoids tautology. The passage also addresses several objections about non-existence, clarifying that non-existence as such does not exist in the mind, and that statements about non-existence must be carefully interpreted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on predication
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the condition for predication: taghayur mafhuman and ittihad hawiyyatan.
Note the example of predication of existence on blackness.
2
Understand the four objections
The commentator lists four objections to a previous statement about non-existence. Identify each objection and its refutation.
The objections are about the expression, the subject matter, existence in the mind, and the status of absolute non-existence.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The passage concludes that the removal of non-existence entails existence but is not identical to existence, preserving the logical distinction.

والقسيمية الرفع بحسب المفهوم، ثم رفع العدم مستلزم للوجود لا نفسه وإن أشعر به كلام الشارح في بحث التقابل والاستلزام لا يقدح في القسمية .
His saying: (and there is not in that a part of a thing being a counterpart to it) because the particular non-existence is not a counterpart of in the removal of the reality of non-existence, and there is no impossibility in it also, since the non-existence of absolute non-existence, inasmuch as it is a removal of absolute non-existence, is a counterpart to it, and inasmuch as it is a particular non-existence, is a part of it. And (the predication) i.e., predicating existence on blackness, is only correct (due to difference in concept) for the concept of blackness is different from the concept of the existent, and the union (in quiddity) i.e., essentially in terms of truth, so there is no lack of benefit here, as in our saying: it is not existent also. And the subsistence of a middle does not follow because non-existence does not admit this division. And it is not a thing: firstly because the expression does not support it, for it would be fitting then that it is not in that a counterpart of a thing being a part of it; secondly because the discussion is about the non-existence of absolute non-existence, and that it is a part of absolute non-existence and a counterpart to it, so the saying that it is not a part of the absolute non-existent has no bearing on what we are discussing; thirdly because the saying that non-existence exists in the mind has no meaning, for non-existences are all among non-existents, as the commentator stated in the discussion of the differentiation of non-existents—yes, after conception it exists in the mind, but the discussion here is about non-existence itself; fourthly because the saying that non-existence is neither non-existent nor existent is only about absolute non-existence, and the discussion here is about the non-existence of absolute non-existence, which is a particular non-existence. His saying: (since the non-existence of absolute non-existence, etc.) means that this restricted thing, inasmuch as it is a non-existence restricted by a restriction, without consideration of the particularity of the restriction, is a type of it, and inasmuch as it is a removal of non-existence, is opposite to it. So the standpoint in the first consideration is that it is a non-existence restricted by a restriction, and then the second consideration is that it is a removal and negation of non-existence. So the position differs according to consideration—thus said some investigators. His saying: (and the union in quiddity). The author said in the discussion of quiddity: the meaning of predicating 'animal' on 'human' is that these two concepts that are different in the mind, their external and imaginary quiddity is one. So neither the unity of two nor the predication of a thing on itself follows. And the commentator said: The mentioned interpretation does not apply in cases like 'human is blind', for there is no quiddity of the concept of 'blind' united with the quiddity of 'human', otherwise it would be an external existent. Therefore, he diverted the text from its apparent meaning and interpreted it according to his chosen view, i.e., the union in quiddity in terms of truth, not that the absolute non-existent is meant by the non-existent in the mind and the external, for non-existence exists in the mind, and because non-existence is not non-existent, otherwise the subsistence of a thing for itself would follow, as it is also not existent. And the subsistence of a middle does not follow, because non-existence does not admit this division, as indicated in the Tajrid. A specific saying: (inasmuch as it is a removal of absolute non-existence). He meant by absolute non-existence the non-existence not attached to anything, not non-existence in the mind and external, i.e., non-existence of mental and external existence. Just as the absolute non-existent is meant by the non-existent in both. Otherwise, it would not be correct for the particular non-existence to be a part of it, for it would not be true of it that it is non-existence of absolute existence; rather, it is non-existence of non-existence. If you say: a part of a thing is affirmative of it, not removal; also the removal of non-existence is existence, and it is not a part of non-existence by intuition. I say: the partiality is affirmation in terms of essence, and the counterpart-hood is removal in terms of concept. Then the removal of non-existence entails existence, not existence itself, even if the commentator's speech in the discussion of opposition and entailment suggests it, it does not harm the partiality.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a clarification of a previous gloss regarding the non-existence of absolute non-existence (adam al-'adam al-mutlaq). The commentator explains that the same thing can be considered from two perspectives: as a part of a genus (qism) when seen as a restricted non-existence, and as an opposite (qasim) when seen as a removal of that genus. This resolves the concern that something cannot be both a part and a counterpart. Then the passage turns to the issue of predication (haml). It explains that predicating existence on blackness is only correct if there is a difference in concept (taghayur mafhuman) but unity in quiddity (ittihad hawiyyatan) in terms of truth. This avoids tautology. The commentator then addresses four objections about non-existence: (1) The expression about 'not being a part' is clarified; (2) The discussion is specifically about adam al-'adam al-mutlaq, not about absolute non-existent; (3) Non-existence as such does not exist in the mind, but its concept does after conception; (4) The statement that non-existence is neither existent nor non-existent applies only to absolute non-existence, not to the particular non-existence under discussion. Finally, the commentary on 'ittihad hawiyyatan' explains that in predications like 'human is blind', there is no real unity of quiddity, so the predication is based on truth (sidq) rather than identity. The passage concludes with a subtle point about the removal of non-existence entailing existence while not being existence itself, preserving the logical distinction.

Key Terms
حمل
مفهوم
هوية
اتحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The removal of non-existence is identical to existence.
Correction: The removal of non-existence entails existence but is not existence itself; it is a logical distinction.

Checkpoint
What is the condition for predicating existence on blackness according to the commentator?
A) The concept of blackness must be identical to the concept of existence.
B) The concept of blackness must be different from the concept of existence, but they must be united in reality.
C) Blackness must be non-existent.
D) Existence must be a part of blackness.

Key Takeaway
Predication requires difference in concept but unity in quiddity; non-existence of non-existence is a removal that entails existence but is not existence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah exists,' do you truly appreciate the logical distinction between the concept of existence and the reality of His being, or do you conflate your mental concept with the divine reality? How might this affect your worship and certainty?
?
The removal of non-existence entails existence but is not existence itself. In your own life, are you satisfied with merely removing vices (non-existence of good) rather than actively cultivating virtues (positive existence)? What would change if you pursued the latter?

Rational Judgments
