Predication as Judgment of Union
Definition and defense of predication as union

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct interpretation of predication (al-haml) in kalam as the judgment of union (al-ittihad) between two things, and how this differs from union in essence.

Core Idea
Predication is not identity; it is union in truth.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif addresses a crucial debate: what does it mean to predicate one thing of another? The commentator defends the view that predication is a judgment of union between subject and predicate, but carefully distinguishes this from union in essence. Grasping this distinction is key to understanding arguments about quiddity, existence, and non-existence in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation. Pay attention to how the author defines predication.
The Arabic is technical; read slowly.
2
Identify the definition of predication
Find where the author states the correct interpretation of predication. Note that he says it is 'the judgment of union between two things'.
3
Understand the distinction between union in predication and union in essence
The author rejects the interpretation that predication is difference in concept and union in truth. He insists that predication itself is the judgment of union, while the concepts remain different. Reflect on why this matters.
This distinction avoids the problem of identity between distinct concepts.
4
Apply to the example of quiddity
Consider how this definition of predication resolves the objection that if existence is other than quiddity, then the existent would subsist in the non-existent. The quiddity in itself is neither existent nor non-existent.
5
Reflect on the broader implications
Think about why establishing this definition is important for defending necessary judgments (al-‘ulūm al-ḍarūriyya) against skeptics.
The lesson connects to the overall goal of kalam: to ground rational theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السواد سواد ولا الحكم بوحدة الاثنين، فهذا جواب عن الدليل الثاني في الشق الأول الذي هو طرف الثبوت من الترديد الأول من الوجه الثالث أعني قوله : وأيضاً فإنه حكم بوحدة الاثنين وترك جواب الدليل الأول في هذا الشق أعني قوله: فهو في نفسه معدوم إلخ اعتمادا على ما سيجيء من أن الماهية في حد ذاتها ليست موجودة ولا معدومة هويته عين هويته، لكن قال المحقق الدواني ناقلاً عن الشيخ : إن الأمور العدمية المحمولة على الشيء متحدة معه بالعرض لكونها منتزعة منه وإن لم تكن متحدة معه حقيقة فتفسير الحمل بالاتحاد بالهوية جار في الذاتيات والعرضيات والأمور العدمية أقول ولعل هذا هو المراد بالاتحاد في الصدق فمرجع التفسيرين واحد. قوله : أي ذاتاً صدقاً عليه فإن قلت الصدق الموصول بعلى معناه الحمل، فيلزم أخذ الحمل في تفسيره قلت : هذا بيان لوجه صحته، وأما تفسيره فهو الحكم بالاتحاد بين الشيئين، وبهذا ظهر أن تفسيره بالتغاير في المفهوم الاتحاد في الصدق كما اختار الشارح فيما سيأتي غير صحيح. قوله : (فهذا جواب عن الدليل أراد بالشق الأول أن يكون المتردد فيه ثبوت الشيء وعدمه في نفسه وقوله : أعني قوله: وأيضاً إلخ بيان للدليل الثاني، وقد عرفت فيما سبق أن الترديد المذكور بقوله : إما نفسه أو غيره بحسب الذات والمفهوم فحاصل الجواب : أن لا نسلم لزوم الحكم بوحدة الاثنين على تقدير المغايرة، لأن المحال إنما هو الحكم بوحدة الاثنين من حيث إنهما اثنان وهاهنا ليس كذلك لأن التغاير من حيث المفهوم، والاتحاد من حيث الهوية، وبهذا ظهر أنه لا يتم الجواب بدون بيان جهتي التغاير والاتحاد . قوله : ( من أن الماهية في حد ذاتها إلخ) بناء على أن شيئاً منهما ليس نفسها ولا داخلاً فيها، فهما مسلوبان عنها في مرتبتها وارتفاع النقيضين في المرتبة جائز، وإذا لم تكن في نفسها معدومة لم يلزم من قيام الوجود بها قيام الموجود بالمعدوم . قوله : ( ولا الحكم بوحدة الاثنين) أي الاتحاد الفاسد وهو اتحاد الاثنين ذاتاً، وأما اتحاد الاثنين اللذين هما المفهومان المتغايران بحسب الذات فلا محذور فيه . قوله : (فهذا جواب عن الدليل الثاني في الشق الأول أراد بالشق الأول أن يكون المراد فيه ثبوت الشيء وعدمه في نفسه، ثم أن كون ما ذكر جواباً عما ذكر، إنما هو إذا لم يكن مراد المستدل بالنفسية والغيرية هما بحسب الخارج، إذ لو أريد ذلك لكان جواباً عن إبطال النفسية، ويكون التقدير والحمل، إنما أفاد التغاير مفهوماً، لكن قوله : والاتحاد هوية لا يخلو عن شائبة اللغوية حينئذ إلا أن يحمل على دفع وهم، فالأظهر أن يراد النفسية بحسب الذات والمفهوم ویراد بغيريتهما الغيرية بحسب مجموعهما لا بحسب كل منهما فتدبر. قوله : ( على ما سيجيء من أن الماهية في حد ذاتها ليست موجودة ولا معدومة قيل : عليه معنى هذا الكلام أن أحدهما ليس عينها ولا داخلاً فيها، لا أنها في نفسها منفكة عن أحدهما، وأنه ليس يلزم من كون الوجود معدوماً اجتماع النقيضين، وقد ذكر في طرف النفي من هذا الترديد أيضاً دليلين قد علم جواب أولهما مما قررناه لك هناك، ومما مر في جواب الأولين من الأربعة وجواب الثاني مما أسلفناه أن من عدم خلو الماهية عن الوجود لا ينافي صدق ترديدها بينه وبين العدم، وهذا أعني قوله والحمل للتغاير بعينه جواب عن الدليل الأول في الشق الأول من الترديد الثاني من الوجه الثالث، كما أن قوله ( والموصوفية) جواب عن الدليل الثاني في هذا الشق أيضاً، وحاصله أن يقال: الموصوفية ونحوها من الأمور الاعتبارية كالإمكان والحدوث والقدم لا وجود لها ولا لنقيضها في الخارج كالامتناع ونقيضه أعني اللاامتناع إذ لا وجود لهما في الخارج بلا شبهة وليس ارتفاع النقيضين بحسب الوجود الخارجي محالاً، إنما المحال ارتفاعهما في الصدق لأن تناقضهما إنما هو باعتباره لا باعتبار الوجود في الخارج، وستفاد أنت فيما يرد عليك من المباحث الآتية زيادة تحقيق تتسلق به إلى الجواب التفصيلي فيما أجبنا عنه إجمالاً، وفيما تركنا جوابه أيضا الشبهة الثانية للقادحين في البديهيات فقط أنا نجزم بالعاديات التي جرت بها العادة كجزمنا بالأوليات التي هي البديهيات سواء لا. قوله : ( اجتماع النقيضين أي اجتماعهما المحال وهو صدقهما على شيء واحد . قوله : ( مما قررناه لك ) بقوله : وجوابه أن ثبوت السواد في الذهن الخ. قوله : (ومما مر إلخ) وهو أن اللازم ثبوت مفهوم المعدوم لا ما صدق عليه، وهذا على تقدير أن يقرر أول الدليلين بقوله وقد يتوهم إلخ . قوله : ( أيضاً) متعلق بقوله جواب. وحينئذ فلم يظهر له فائدة كثيرة والجواب أن له فائدة تامة وهي دفع الدليل المذكور، لأن العدم إذا لم يكن نفس الماهية ولا داخلا فيها لم يستقم أن يقال: إذا كان الوجود غير الماهية يلزم قيام الموجود بالمعدوم ، وإنما يلزم إذا كان العدم نفسها أو داخلاً فيها، وإلا فلا يلزم من مغايرة الوجود لها اتصافها بنقيضه، أعني العدم حال اتصافها به فتأمل. قوله: (ومما مر في جواب إلخ هذا على التوهم الذي ذكره هناك، وهو أن ترجع الضمائر منه إلى نفي الوجود، وقد نبهناك على جواب آخر فتدبر. قوله : ( في هذا الشق أيضاً أي كما أن الأول جواب بعينه، أو كما أن الدليل الأول في الشق الثاني أو في هذا الشق من الوجه الثالث، كما أن الشق الثاني المذكور أولاً. قوله : (كجزمنا بالأوليات) قد يمنع هذا للفرق الظاهر بينهما كما يشهد به صريح العقل وقد اندفع بما ذكره في تحقيق الحد المختار للعلم، وأشار هاهنا أيضاً، نعم لنا أن نقول: فالجزم بالحسيات أيضاً كذلك فلم يقولون بها.
Black is black, and not the judgment of the union of the two. So this is a response to the second proof in the first branch, which is the side of affirmation in the first alternation of the third aspect, namely his statement: 'And also, he judged the union of the two.' And he left the response to the first proof in this branch, namely his statement: 'So it is in itself non-existent, etc.', relying on what will come that quiddity (al-mahiya) in its essence is neither existent nor non-existent, its identity is identical to its identity. But the investigator al-Dawwani said, quoting from the Shaykh: 'Non-existential matters predicated upon a thing are accidentally united with it because they are derived from it, even if they are not truly united with it. So the interpretation of predication (al-haml) as union in identity (al-ittihad bi al-huwiyya) applies to essential (al-dhatiyyat) and accidental (al-'aradiyyat) matters and non-existential matters.' I say: perhaps this is what is meant by union in predication (al-ittihad fi al-sidq), so the reference of the two interpretations is one. His statement: 'i.e., in essence, truly predicated upon it.' For if you say 'predication connected with 'ala' means predication, then it would require taking predication in its interpretation.' I say: 'This is an explanation of its correctness. As for its interpretation, it is the judgment of union between two things. By this it becomes apparent that its interpretation as difference in concept (al-taghayur fi al-mafhum) and union in truth as the commentator chose later is not correct.' His statement: 'So this is a response to the proof' – he intended by the first branch that the matter under dispute is the existence of the thing and its non-existence in itself. And his statement: 'namely his statement: And also, etc.' is an explanation of the second proof. And you have known previously that the mentioned alternation by his statement: 'either itself or other' is in terms of essence (al-dhat) and concept (al-mafhum). The summary of the response is: we do not grant the necessity of the judgment of the union of the two on the assumption of difference (al-mughayara), because the impossible is only the judgment of the union of the two insofar as they are two, and here it is not so, because difference is in terms of concept, and union is in terms of identity. By this it becomes apparent that the response is not complete without clarifying the aspects of difference and union. His statement: 'that quiddity in its essence, etc.' is based on the fact that neither of them is itself nor internal to it, so both are negated of it in its level, and the lifting of two contradictories in the level is permissible. And if it is not in itself non-existent, then from the subsistence of existence in it it does not follow that the existent subsists in the non-existent. His statement: 'and not the judgment of the union of the two' meaning the corrupt union, which is the union of two things in essence. As for the union of two things which are two differing concepts in terms of essence, there is no objection to it. His statement: 'So this is a response to the second proof in the first branch' – he intended by the first branch that what is meant in it is the existence of the thing and its non-existence in itself. Then, that what was mentioned is a response to what was mentioned only if the proponent intended by 'being itself' and 'being other' externally. For if that were intended, it would be a response to the denial of being itself, and the estimation and predication would only yield difference conceptually, but his statement 'and union is identity' would not be free from a trace of redundancy then, unless it is interpreted as repelling an illusion. So the apparent is that 'being itself' is intended in terms of essence and concept, and 'their being other' is intended in terms of their totality, not each individually, so reflect. His statement: 'upon what will come that quiddity in its essence is neither existent nor non-existent' – it was said: the meaning of this statement is that one of them is not identical to it nor internal to it, not that it is in itself detached from either of them. And that it does not follow from existence being non-existent the conjunction of two contradictories. And he mentioned on the side of negation of this alternation also two proofs, the response to the first of which you know from what we established for you there, and from what passed in the response to the first two of the four, and the response to the second from what we previously stated: that the lack of the quiddity's being devoid of existence does not contradict the truth of its alternation between it and non-existence. And this, namely his statement: 'and predication is for difference' is itself a response to the first proof in the first branch of the second alternation of the third aspect, just as his statement: 'and being described' is a response to the second proof in this branch as well. Its summary is: it is said: 'being described' and similar matters that are accidental (al-umur al-i'tibariyya) like possibility (al-imkan), origination (al-huduth), and pre-eternity (al-qidam) have no existence nor the existence of their opposite in the external world, like impossibility (al-imtina') and its opposite, namely non-impossibility (al-la imtina'), for they have no existence in the external world without doubt. And the lifting of two contradictories in terms of external existence is not impossible; the impossible is their lifting in predication (al-sidq), because their contradictoriness is considered in terms of it, not in terms of existence in the external world. And you will benefit from what comes to you in subsequent investigations additional verification by which you may ascend to the detailed response to what we answered in summary, and also to what we left unanswered. The second doubt for those who object to necessary sciences only: we are certain of habitual things (al-'adiyyat) that are customary, like our certainty of first principles (al-awwaliyyat) which are necessary sciences, whether not. His statement: 'conjunction of two contradictories' meaning their impossible conjunction, which is their predication of one thing. His statement: 'from what we established for you' by his statement: 'and its response is that the subsistence of blackness in the mind, etc.' His statement: 'and from what passed, etc.' it is that the necessary is the subsistence of the concept of the non-existent, not its reference. And this is on the assumption that the first of the two proofs is formulated by his statement: 'and it may be imagined, etc.' His statement: 'also' is connected to his statement 'response'. And then it does not appear to have much benefit, and the response is that it has complete benefit, which is repelling the mentioned proof, because if non-existence is not the quiddity itself nor internal to it, it cannot be said: 'if existence is other than the quiddity, then it would follow that the existent subsists in the non-existent.' It only follows if non-existence is itself or internal to it. Otherwise, it does not follow from the otherness of existence to it that it is qualified by its opposite, namely non-existence at the time of its being qualified by it, so reflect. His statement: 'and from what passed in the response, etc.' this is on the assumption that he mentioned there, namely that the pronouns from it refer to the negation of existence, and we have alerted you to another response, so reflect. His statement: 'in this branch also' i.e., just as the first is itself a response, or just as the first proof in the second branch or in this branch of the third aspect, as the second branch was mentioned first. His statement: 'like our certainty of first principles' – this may be denied due to the apparent difference between them as attested by pure reason, and it has been rebutted by what he mentioned in the verification of the chosen definition of knowledge, and he pointed to it here as well. Yes, we can say: our certainty of sensory things is likewise, so why do they not accept them?
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of a detailed commentary on responses to arguments concerning necessary sciences. The commentator explains the response to the second proof regarding the union of two things, clarifying that union and difference are considered from the perspectives of essence and concept, not just external existence. The argument that quiddity in itself is neither existent nor non-existent is defended, and the objection that predication requires identity is addressed by distinguishing between union in reality and union in predication. The commentator also addresses the second doubt raised by critics of necessary sciences, affirming that habitual certainties are not equivalent to first principles. Throughout, the emphasis is on the correct interpretation of predication, union, and the role of quiddity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That predication (al-ḥaml) means the subject and predicate are identical in essence (al-dhāt).
Correction: Predication is the judgment of union between two things in terms of truth (al-ṣidq), not identity in essence. The concepts remain distinct.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct interpretation of predication (al-haml)?
A) It is the judgment of union between two things.
B) It is the identity of the subject and predicate in essence.
C) It is the difference in concept only.
D) It is the judgment of existence in the external world.

Key Takeaway
Predication is the judgment of union between two things, not identity in essence; this distinction is crucial for avoiding philosophical contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is Merciful,' do you secretly assume that 'Merciful' is just a label for an aspect of His essence, reducing predication to identity? How does the definition of predication as judgment of union force you to think differently about God's names?
?
Have you ever caught yourself treating theological statements as mere statements about the same reality using different words, like saying 'water' and 'H2O'? How does this lesson expose the error in that view and what must change in how you approach divine attributes?
?
If predication is union in the mind, not in reality, then every time you say 'God is Knower,' you are performing an act of mental union that should humble you—recognizing that your finite mind is joining two concepts that are infinitely distinct. How does this change the reverence you bring to such statements?

Refuting the Contradiction Argument Through Mental Considerations
The author refutes a proof that existence-essence distinction implies contradiction by appealing to mental considerations and the nature of contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence-essence distinction does not lead to a contradiction, because contradiction is about truth in judgment, not about external existence.

Core Idea
Contradiction is about truth, not about existence in the external world.

This passage addresses a key objection: if existence is separate from essence, wouldn't that imply a contradiction? The author shows that this objection confuses the nature of contradiction, which pertains to judgments, not to external things. He explains that concepts like possibility are mental considerations without external reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the main refutation. Notice how the author connects the discussion to previous arguments.
The passage is dense and references earlier points; focus on the core claim.
2
Identify the key distinction
The author distinguishes between external existence and truth in judgment. Find where he says contradiction is about الصدق (truth) and not about الوجود الخارجي (external existence).
This is the heart of the refutation.
3
Understand mental considerations
The author points out that attributes like possibility and origination are mental considerations (اعتبارية). They have no external existence, so their opposites also lack external existence. Thus, no contradiction arises from their combined absence in the external world.
This shows the objection mistakenly assumes external existence for mental concepts.

Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument that if existence is not identical to essence, then there would be a contradiction. The author states that it is not necessary that existence being non-existent (i.e., existence being other than essence) leads to the combination of two contradictories. He mentions that two proofs were given on the side of negation, and the answers to them are known from previous discussions. The first proof is answered by the point that attribution indicates distinction; the second proof is answered by the point that 'being described' is a mental consideration. The summary of the answer is that things like possibility, origination, and eternity are mental considerations; neither they nor their opposites have external existence. Therefore, the simultaneous absence of both in external existence is not impossible; impossibility only applies to truth in judgment, because contradictoriness is about judgment, not external existence. The author then promises further detailed verification in later discussions. The commentary glosses key phrases: 'combination of the two contradictories' means their being true of one thing; the benefit of the answer is to repulse the proof that if existence is other than essence, then a existent would subsist through non-existence. This is only required if non-existence were the essence or internal to it; otherwise distinction does not entail being described by its opposite.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If existence is not identical to essence, then a thing would be both existent and non-existent simultaneously, which is contradictory.
Correction: The contradiction would only occur if existence and non-existence were both true in judgment. But mental considerations like possibility have no external existence, so their opposites also lack external existence. Therefore, no contradiction arises from their absence in the external world.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is the simultaneous absence of two contradictories in external existence not impossible?
A) Because contradiction only applies to mental concepts.
B) Because external existence does not allow for contraries.
C) Because contradictoriness is about truth in judgment, not external existence.
D) Because non-existence is not a real entity.

Key Takeaway
The existence-essence distinction does not lead to contradiction because contradiction applies to truth in judgment, not to the external existence of mental considerations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reason about theological truths, do you often treat mental constructs (like 'possibility' or 'being described') as if they had concrete reality? How might that lead you to false conclusions about God?
?
Have you ever clung to a logical objection because it felt convincing, without examining whether the contradiction you perceived was about truth or about existence? What does that reveal about your intellectual humility?
?
The passage distinguishes between judgment and external reality. In your own life, where do you conflate the two—believing that because something is absent from your thoughts, it is absent from reality, or vice versa? How will you correct that starting today?

Refutation of the Objection that Customary Propositions Equal Self-Evident Truths
Presentation and refutation of the second doubt against self-evident truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why customary propositions, despite producing similar psychological certainty, are epistemically inferior to self-evident truths due to the possibility of their opposite.

Core Idea
Certainty from habit is not the same as certainty from necessity.

This passage addresses a critical challenge against the reliability of self-evident truths. The objector argues that we feel just as certain about everyday regularities (like an old man not being born old) as we do about logical axioms, yet we do not rely on those regularities—so why should we rely on self-evident truths? The refutation clarifies the fundamental epistemic difference: self-evident truths cannot be conceived otherwise, whereas customary truths are merely habitual.

Guided Path
1
Read the objector's claim and examples
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Note the examples given: the old man, the vessels not turning into scholars, etc. Observe that the objector claims equal certainty.
The objector uses vivid examples to make the point relatable.
2
Understand the refutation's first point
The commentary responds that if 'no difference' means no difference in the essence of certainty, that is granted but does not imply equal lack of reliance. The key is the rank of certainty.
The author's chosen definition of knowledge already addresses this.
3
Grasp the key distinction: possibility of the opposite
Self-evident truths cannot have their opposite possible in essence (impossible to conceive the opposite), while customary propositions can. This is the fundamental epistemic difference.
This is the core of the refutation.
4
Note the commentary on sense-perception
The examples involve sense-perception for the subject, but the judgment itself is not derived from sense; it is a rational inference from habit. This further weakens the analogy.
The detailed breakdown of life stages illustrates the customary nature but does not affect the epistemological argument.

الشبهة ( الثانية ) للقادحين في البديهيات فقط ( أنا نجزم بالعاديات ) التي جرت بها العادة كجزمنا بالأوليات التي هي البديهيات ( سواء لا فرق بينهما فيما يعود إلى الجزم وطمأنينة العقل مع أن العاديات لا اعتماد عليها، فكذا البديهيات ( فمنها ) أي من العاديات المجزوم بها أن هذا الشيخ الذي رأيناه الآن على هيئة الشيخوخة (لم يتولد دفعة) على هذه الهيئة ( بلا أب وأم بل) تولد منهما ملتبساً (بالتدريج، فكان وليداً ثم طفلاً ثم مترعرعاً) من ترعرع الصبي أي تحرك ونشأ ( إلى أن شاخ ) بعد الشباب والكهولة ) ومنها أن أواني البيت لم تنقلب بعد خروجي عنه أناساً فضلاء محققين في العلوم الإلهية والهندسية، ولا أحجاره ) أي ولم تنقلب أحجار البيت ) جواهر) نفيسة ( و ) لا ماء ( البحر) الذي رأيناه من قبل ( دهناً وعسلاً و) إن (ليس تحت رجلي ) الآن ( ياقوتة من ألف من ومنها أن المجيب عن خطابي بما يطابقه حي فاهم لما خوطب به ) عالم بما يطابقه من الجواب
قوله : (كجزمنا بالأوليات) قد يمنع هذا للفرق الظاهر بينهما كما يشهد به صريح العقل وقد اندفع بما ذكره في تحقيق الحد المختار للعلم، وأشار هاهنا أيضاً، نعم لنا أن نقول: فالجزم بالحسيات أيضاً كذلك فلم يقولون بها.
قوله : ( لا فرق بينهما إلخ ) يرد عليه أنه : إن أريد به عدم الفرق في أصل الجزم، وعدم احتمال النقيض فمسلم، لكن لا يستلزم ذلك التساوي بينهما في عدم الاعتماد، وإن أريد به عدمه في مرتبة الجزم وخصوصيته فممنوع فإن الأوليات لا يمكن نقيضها إمكاناً ذاتياً بخلاف العاديات .
قوله : إن هذا الشيخ إلخ المحكوم عليه في هذه القضية وإن كان من الحسيات لكن ( الحكم ليس منها ، إذ لم يستند ذلك إلى الحس، وكذا في قوله : أن ابني هذا ليس بجبريل، فما قيل : المناسب إسقاط لفظ هذا حتى لا يكون من الحسيات، إذ هم قائلون بها، وكون القضية منها يقتضي القدح فيها أيضاً ليس بشيء.
قوله : ( فكان وليداً) أي مولوداً ثم طفلاً، الأسنان أربعة سن النمو، ويسمى سن وهو إلى قريب من ثلاثين سنة، ثم سن الوقوف وهو سن الشباب وهو إلى نحو من خمس وثلاثين سنة أو أربعين ثم سن الانحطاط مع بقاء القوة وهو سن الكهولة، وهو إلى نحو من ستين سنة، ثم سن الانحطاط مع ظهور ضعف في القوة سن الشيخوخة إلى آخر العمر، وسن الحداثة ينقسم إلى سن الطفولة وهو أن يكون المولود غير مستعد الأعضاء للحركة والنهوض، ثم وهو بعد النهوض وقبل الشدة، وهو أن يكون الأسنان قد استوفت السقوط والنبات، ثم سن سن الترعرع وهو بعد الشدة ونبات الأسنان قبل المراهقة، ثم سن الغلامية والرهاق إلى أن يبقل ،وجهه ثم سن الفتى إلى أن يقف النمو .
قوله : ( أي تحرك ونشأ مدته في الأغلب إلى ثمانية وعشرين وقيل إلى خمسة وثلاثين بدليل زيادة الجمال والقوة وعود الطواحين الساقطة بعد العشرين وأما مدة الكهولة وهي التي يكون النقصان فيها خفياً، فهي من خمسة وثلاثين وقيل من أربعين إلى ستين، ومدة الشيخوخة وهي التي يكون النقصان فيها ظاهراً من آخر الكهولة إلى ما يشاء الله تعالى، وتفصيله موكول إلى موضعه .
The second doubt, for the critics of self-evident truths only: 'We are certain of customary things' which are established by habit, just as we are certain of primary truths which are the self-evident ones, with no difference between them regarding certainty and tranquility of the mind, while customary things are not relied upon, so likewise self-evident truths. For among them, i.e., among the customary things that are certain, is that this old man whom we see now in the state of old age was not generated instantly in this state without father and mother, but rather was generated from them gradually, being a newborn, then a child, then a boy becoming restless and growing, until he became old after youth and middle age. And among them is that the vessels of the house, after I left it, did not turn into virtuous people who are investigators in divine and geometrical sciences, nor did its stones turn into precious jewels, nor did the sea water which we saw before turn into oil and honey, and that there is not under my feet now a ruby worth a thousand dinars. And among them is that the respondent to my speech with a corresponding answer is living, understanding what was addressed to him, knowing the answer that corresponds to it. His saying: 'just as we are certain of primary truths' — this may be denied due to the apparent difference between them as witnessed by clear reason, and it has been repelled by what he mentioned in the verification of the chosen definition of knowledge, and he indicated it here as well. Indeed, we can say: and certainty in sense-perceptions is also like that, so why do they not say it? His saying: 'no difference between them etc.' is responded to: if what is meant is no difference in the essence of certainty and the absence of possibility of the opposite, then it is granted, but that does not entail their equality in the lack of reliance; and if what is meant is no difference in the rank of certainty and its specificity, then it is prevented, because primary truths cannot have their opposite possible in essence, unlike customary things. His saying: 'this old man etc.' — the subject in this proposition, although it is from sense-perceptions, but the judgment is not from them, because it is not based on sense. Similarly, his saying: 'this son of mine is not Jibril' — so what is said: it is appropriate to drop the word 'this' so that it is not from sense-perceptions, since they affirm them, and the proposition being from them necessitates objection to it as well — is not correct. His saying: 'so he was a newborn' i.e., born, then a child. The stages of life are four: the stage of growth, called youth, up to near thirty years; then the stage of stability, which is the stage of youth, up to about thirty-five or forty; then the stage of decline with remaining strength, which is the stage of middle age, up to about sixty; then the stage of decline with the appearance of weakness in strength, the stage of old age, to the end of life. The stage of childhood is divided into: the stage of infancy, when the newborn is not ready in organs for movement and rising; then after rising and before strength, when the teeth have completed falling and growing; then the stage of movement and growth, after strength and the growth of teeth before puberty; then the stage of boyhood and approaching maturity until his face shows down; then the stage of youth until growth stops. His saying: 'i.e., he moved and grew; its duration is mostly up to twenty-eight, and it is said up to thirty-five, evidenced by increase in beauty and strength and the return of fallen molars after twenty. As for the duration of middle age, in which decline is hidden, it is from thirty-five, and it is said from forty, to sixty. And the duration of old age, in which decline is apparent, is from the end of middle age to whatever Allah wills. Its detail is referred to its place.'
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the second doubt against self-evident truths. The objector claims that we are just as certain of customary regularities (like a man not being born old) as we are of primary self-evident truths (like the whole being greater than the part). They argue that since we feel the same degree of certainty and peace of mind, but customary propositions are not considered reliable, then self-evident ones should also be unreliable. They give examples: an old man was not born old; house vessels did not turn into scholars; stones did not become jewels; sea water did not turn into oil; there is no ruby underfoot; and a respondent who answers appropriately is alive and understanding. The refutation, given in the commentary, first notes that some may deny the claim of equal certainty due to the apparent difference. The author's chosen definition of knowledge already addresses this. The commentator then responds: if by 'no difference' they mean no difference in the basic certainty and the impossibility of the opposite, that is granted, but that does not imply equal lack of reliance; if they mean no difference in the rank of certainty, that is denied because primary truths cannot have their opposite possible (impossible in essence), unlike customary matters. Furthermore, the commentary points out that the subject of the proposition (e.g., 'this old man') is from sense-perceptions, but the judgment about gradual development is not based on sense alone; it is a rational inference from habit. The detailed breakdown of the stages of human life illustrates the customary nature of the examples but does not affect the epistemological argument.

الأوليات

الاعتماد
الإمكان الذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That equal psychological certainty implies equal epistemic strength and unreliability.
Correction: Psychological certainty does not determine epistemic necessity; self-evident truths are necessary (impossible to conceive the opposite), while customary truths are contingent.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between self-evident truths and customary propositions according to the refutation?
A) Self-evident truths are known by the senses, while customary ones are known by reason.
B) Self-evident truths cannot have their opposite possible, while customary propositions can.
C) Self-evident truths are not reliable, while customary ones are.
D) Self-evident truths produce less certainty than customary ones.

Key Takeaway
Self-evident truths are necessary and their opposite is impossible in essence, while customary propositions are contingent, making the two categories epistemically distinct despite similar psychological certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You feel absolute certainty about many things—that the sun will rise, that your parents raised you, that a chair will hold you. According to this lesson, are you justified in relying on that certainty as you do for Allah's existence? Where have you unconsciously treated habit as necessity?
?
The objector claims peace of mind is the same for customary and self-evident truths. Think of a belief you hold with deep tranquility—perhaps about your own goodness or your understanding of a person. Could that tranquility be merely the comfort of habit rather than the grip of truth? How would you know?
?
The commentary reveals that the judgment about the old man is not direct sense but rational inference. Now, examine a strong conviction you have about another person's character—is it based on direct perception or a chain of habitual inferences? If the latter, what does that imply about the certainty you afford it?

Distinction Between Necessary and Customary Sciences
Jurjani distinguishes necessary sciences from customary sciences with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between necessary sciences (badihiyyat) and customary sciences (adiyyat) and why they are not equal in certainty.

Core Idea
Customary judgments are certain in practice, but not in principle.

This passage from Jurjani's commentary distinguishes between necessary sciences, which are self-evident and cannot be conceived otherwise, and customary sciences, which are based on repeated experience and could in principle be otherwise. The author gives vivid examples of customary judgments and then addresses a potential objection that there is no difference in certainty between the two types.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic text slowly, then follow the literal translation. Notice the examples given and the structure of the argument.
Pay attention to the examples: the old man, the house vessels, the sea, the ruby.
2
Identify the examples of customary judgments
List the examples Jurjani provides for customary judgments. Ask yourself why each is considered certain but not necessary.
These examples illustrate the 'customary' category.
3
Understand the rebuttal of the counterclaim
Analyze the response to the objection 'There is no difference between them.' Distinguish between the essence of certainty (which is shared) and the degree of certainty (which differs).
The key is that necessary sciences cannot be otherwise, while customary ones can, even if we are normally certain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمنها ) أي من العاديات المجزوم بها أن هذا الشيخ الذي رأيناه الآن على هيئة الشيخوخة (لم يتولد دفعة) على هذه الهيئة ( بلا أب وأم بل) تولد منهما ملتبساً (بالتدريج، فكان وليداً ثم طفلاً ثم مترعرعاً) من ترعرع الصبي أي تحرك ونشأ ( إلى أن شاخ ) بعد الشباب والكهولة ) ومنها أن أواني البيت لم تنقلب بعد خروجي عنه أناساً فضلاء محققين في العلوم الإلهية والهندسية، ولا أحجاره ) أي ولم تنقلب أحجار البيت ) جواهر) نفيسة ( و ) لا ماء ( البحر) الذي رأيناه من قبل ( دهناً وعسلاً و) إن (ليس تحت رجلي ) الآن ( ياقوتة من ألف من ومنها أن المجيب عن خطابي بما يطابقه حي فاهم لما خوطب به ) عالم بما يطابقه من الجواب قوله : ( لا فرق بينهما إلخ ) يرد عليه أنه : إن أريد به عدم الفرق في أصل الجزم، وعدم احتمال النقيض فمسلم، لكن لا يستلزم ذلك التساوي بينهما في عدم الاعتماد، وإن أريد به عدمه في مرتبة الجزم وخصوصيته فممنوع فإن الأوليات لا يمكن نقيضها إمكاناً ذاتياً بخلاف العاديات .
Among them (i.e., among the customary judgments that are regarded as certain) are: that this old man whom we see now in the form of old age was not born suddenly in that form without father and mother, but rather was generated from them gradually, so he was a newborn, then a child, then a growing boy (from tar'ara'a al-sabi meaning he moved and grew) until he became old (after youth and manhood); and that the vessels of the house have not turned into excellent, accomplished men in the divine and geometrical sciences after I left, nor have its stones turned into precious jewels, nor has the water of the sea which we saw before become oil and honey, nor is there under my feet now a ruby of a thousand; and that the respondent to my speech with a matching response is living, understanding what he is addressed with, and knowing the matching answer. His statement: 'There is no difference between them, etc.' is rebutted: if what is intended is no difference in the essence of certainty and in the impossibility of the opposite, it is granted, but that does not entail equality between them in lack of reliance; and if what is intended is no difference in the degree and particularity of certainty, it is denied, because primary propositions cannot have their opposite in a self-possible manner, unlike customary ones.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument distinguishing necessary sciences (badihiyyat) from customary sciences (adiyyat). The author (Jurjani) begins by giving examples of empirical judgments that are commonly considered certain but are actually based on habit, not necessity: an old man was not born suddenly, household items do not transform, a speaker is alive and understanding. Then he addresses a counterclaim that there is no difference between necessary and customary sciences. He argues that if the claim means they are equal in the essence of certainty and impossibility of the opposite, then it is granted but irrelevant; but if it means they are equal in the degree of certainty, it is denied because first principles cannot be conceived otherwise in themselves, while customary matters can. Thus the distinction remains: customary judgments are not truly necessary sciences.

Key Terms
البديهيات
العاديات
الجزم
إمكان ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that customary judgments are as certain as first principles because they seem undeniable in everyday life.
Correction: The text shows that customary judgments are certain in practice but can be conceived otherwise, unlike necessary sciences which cannot be imagined false.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why are customary judgments not equivalent to necessary sciences in certainty?
A) Because customary judgments are based on sense perception while necessary sciences are based on reason.
B) Because necessary sciences cannot have their opposite in a self-possible manner, but customary judgments can.
C) Because necessary sciences are known by all humans while customary judgments are not.
D) Because customary judgments involve probability while necessary sciences are absolute.

Key Takeaway
Customary judgments are certain in everyday life but remain logically possible to be false, unlike necessary sciences which are self-evidently true.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

You assume the sun will rise tomorrow, but what if it doesn't? Would your certainty about God's power be shaken, or do you merely rely on habit?
?
This lesson says customary certainties are only certain 'in practice'. How many of your daily acts of worship rest on such customary certainties rather than necessary truths? Does that concern you?
?
The objector claims no difference between necessary and customary certainty. Are there beliefs you hold that you treat as necessary but are actually only customary? What would it cost you to admit that?

Why Customary Judgments Lack Certainty
Jurjani argues that empirical judgments are uncertain due to possible divine or celestial causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why empirical judgments are considered uncertain according to both the mutakallimun and the philosophers, and how an objection from the Quran is answered.

Core Idea
Empirical judgments remain uncertain because divine or celestial causes could produce rare events.

In this passage, Jurjani moves from examples to a general argument: all empirical propositions are open to the possibility of error. He explains this from two perspectives—that of the mutakallimun, who ground all events in God's free will, and that of the philosophers, who ground events in celestial configurations. He then addresses an objection based on the Quranic claim that God's custom does not change, showing that the verse is open to interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the full English translation to get the gist of the argument.
Notice the two distinct explanations for uncertainty.
2
Identify the two explanations
Look for the sections beginning with 'أما عند المتكلمين' and 'أما عند الحكماء'. Understand how each group accounts for the possibility of rare events.
The mutakallimun appeal to God's free agency; the philosophers appeal to celestial configurations.
3
Analyze the objection and answer
Find the objection (قيل) and the answer (وأجيب). Note that the objection cites a Quranic verse, and the answer argues that the verse's meaning is not definitive.
The answer uses the concept of 'ظني الدلالة' (probable indication).
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how the author concludes that empirical judgments are not certain. Think about why this matters for theology and epistemology.
This is a key argument against relying on empirical observations for certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قادر على التعبير عنه ثم إذا تأملنا في هذه القضايا التي ذكرناها لم نجدها مما يجوز الجزم بها فكان الاحتمال أي احتمال الخطأ قائماً في الكل أي في كل هذه القضايا باتفاق العقلاء أما عند المتكلمين فلاستناد الكل أي كل الأشياء عندهم إلى القادر المختار فلعله أوجب أي أثبت وأوجد باختياره شيئاً من ذلك أي مما ذكر من الشيخ المتولد دفعة ونظائره من الأمور المستبعدة التي لم تجربها عادته للإمكان فإن هذه الأمور المستبعدة جداً ممكنة في حد ذواتها قطعاً وعموم القدرة لجميع الممكنات مستقربة كانت أو مستبعدة وأما عند الحكماء فلاستناد الحوادث الأرضية عندهم إلى الأوضاع الفلكية الحادثة من حركاتها فلعله حدث شكل أي وضع غريب فلكي لم يقع فيما مضى من الزمان مثله أو وقع لكنه لا يتكرر ذلك الشكل بتعاقب الأمثال إلا في ألوف من السنين كثيرة جدا بحيث لا تفي بضبطها التواريخ فاقتضى ذلك الشكل الغريب ذلك الأمر العجيب قوله : ( أما عند المتكلمين فلاستناد الكل عندهم إلى القادر المختار) قيل : عليه التمسك بالاستناد إلى القادر المختار غير صحيح، لأن المتكلمين قائلون بأن عادة الله في خلق الإنسان ذلك التدريج، وقد قال عز من قائل ﴿ولن تجد لسنة الله تبديلاً﴾ [الأحزاب: ٦٢ ]، وأجيب بأن هذا دليل نقلي قطعي الثبوت لكنه ظني الدلالة، فلا يفيد القطع بالتدريج في الخلق لأن يمكن الإضمار بأن يقال : التقدير ولن تجد لسنة الله تبديلاً إلا إذا أراد تبديله بخرق عادته .
He is able to express it. Then, when we reflect on these propositions that we have mentioned, we do not find them to be such that certainty is permissible regarding them. Thus possibility—i.e., the possibility of error—subsists in all of them, in all these propositions, by agreement of the rational people. For the mutakallimun, because all things are based, in their view, on the free Agent (al-Qadir al-Mukhtar), so perhaps He necessitated (i.e., established and created by His choice) something of that, i.e., of what was mentioned, such as the old man born suddenly and similar improbable things that His custom has not tried; because these very improbable things are certainly possible in themselves, and the universality of power extends to all possibles, whether they are familiar or improbable. For the philosophers, because earthly events are based, in their view, on celestial configurations arising from their movements; so perhaps a strange celestial configuration occurred—a position that has not occurred in past time like it, or it occurred but that configuration does not recur by succession of similars except in very many thousands of years such that the histories do not suffice to encompass them; so that strange configuration necessitates that wondrous thing. His statement: 'For the mutakallimun, because all things are based on the free Agent'—it is said against this: relying on the free Agent is not valid, because the mutakallimun say that God's custom in creating man is gradual, and He said, the Mighty and Majestic: 'And you will never find in the way of Allah any change' [Quran 33:62]. And it is answered that this is a transmitted proof whose transmission is definitive but whose indication is probable, so it does not yield certainty about gradual creation, because it is possible that there is an ellipsis: the estimation is 'You will never find in the way of Allah any change unless He intends to change it by breaking His custom.'
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a proof that customary empirical judgments are not certain. The author first states that when we examine the earlier examples, we do not find them worthy of certainty; rather, the possibility of error remains in all of them by consensus of rational people. He then gives two explanations: For the mutakallimun, since all things depend on the free Agent, God could will a rare event like an old man being born suddenly. Although such events are improbable, they are intrinsically possible and within God's power. For the philosophers, earthly events depend on celestial configurations, and a rare astronomical alignment could produce a wondrous event that seems contrary to habit. The author then addresses an objection: The mutakallimun might argue that God's custom is stable, citing the Quranic verse that God's way does not change. The answer is that the verse is a transmitted proof whose meaning is not definitive; it can be interpreted as meaning that God's way does not change unless He decides to break His custom. Thus, the possibility remains, and empirical judgments are not certain.

القادر المختار
الفلاسفة
الأوضاع الفلكية
السنة الله

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Quranic verse 'And you will never find in the way of Allah any change' proves that empirical regularities are certain and never broken.
Correction: The author argues that the verse is 'zanni al-dalala' (probable in indication) and can be interpreted with an ellipsis, meaning that God's custom does not change unless He intends to break it. Thus, it does not provide absolute certainty.

Checkpoint

B) Because the mutakallimun hold that everything depends on God's free choice, so He could create a rare event.
C) Because the mutakallimun believe in fixed natural laws that never change.
D) Because the mutakallimun rely on sense perception which is fallible.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of rare events, whether from divine will or celestial configurations, prevents certainty in empirical judgments.

Do you truly live as if your next breath is dependent on Allah's direct will, or do you subconsciously rely on habit? What can you do today to remind yourself of this dependence?
?
The philosophers attribute earthly events to celestial configurations—forces outside your control. But this lesson shows even those can produce unprecedented surprises. Do you sometimes feel trapped by 'natural laws' or 'fate'? How does knowing that Allah alone is the true disposer of affairs free you from that feeling? Commit to one specific situation this week where you will actively rely on Allah for the outcome.
?

But Jurjani shows this verse allows exceptions. Is there an area of your life where you have unconsciously limited Allah's power by assuming His custom must hold? Objection and Answer: Does Angelic Transformation Invalidate Empirical Certainty?
Jurjani rebuts the objection that empirical judgments are invalidated by angelic transformation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun defend the reliability of empirical judgments against the objection that angels can change form.

Core Idea
Empirical certainty about individuals is based on relationship, not just appearance.

This passage addresses a powerful objection: if an angel can appear in human form, how can I be certain my son is not Jibril? Jurjani shows that the judgment is not purely sensory but involves the relationship of sonhood, rescuing empirical propositions from the threat of angelic transformation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the objector's challenge and Jurjani's response.
2
Identify the key reformulation
Jurjani suggests dropping the word 'this' and instead referring to 'my son' as the one with the relationship of son. Understand why this change matters.
3
Reflect on the implications for empirical knowledge
Consider how this answer preserves the distinction between necessary and customary judgments. The proposition about one's son is not a pure sensory proposition but a relational one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إنما فصل هذه القضايا السابقة لأن المتكلم قائل بوقوع ما هو قادح فيها أعني تبديل صورة الملك فأنا أجزم بأن ابني هذا ليس جبريل وكذا الذبابة التي نراها ليست جبريل وأنتم يا أهل الملة تجوزونه أي تجوزون ما ذكر من كون ابني أو الذبابة جبريل إذ نقلتم أنه كان يظهر جبريل تارة في صورة دحية الكلبي وكان قوله : ( بأن ابني هذا ليس جبريل قيل : المناسب أن يسقط لفظ هذا، ويقال : أنا أجزم بأن ابني أي من حكم بكونه ابني، ووصف ببنوتي وولدي وهو على صورته وصفته الآن ليس بجبريل، لا تكون القضية من الحسيات، إذ هم قائلون بالحسيات وكون القضية منها يقتضي القدح حتى فيها أيضاً.
Also, he only detailed these previous propositions because the mutakallim says that something that invalidates them occurs, namely the transformation of an angel's form. I am certain that this son of mine is not Jibril, and likewise the fly that we see is not Jibril. But you, O people of the religious community, permit it—i.e., you permit what was mentioned, that my son or the fly could be Jibril—since you reported that Jibril used to appear sometimes in the form of Dihya al-Kalbi. His statement: 'that this son of mine is not Jibril'—it is said: it is appropriate to drop the word 'this', and say: I am certain that my son—i.e., the one considered to be my son, described as my son and offspring, and who is now in his current form and description—is not Jibril. Then the proposition would not be among the sensory propositions, because they (the mutakallimun) affirm sensory propositions, and if the proposition were among them, it would necessitate a defect even in them.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the certainty of empirical judgments. The objection is: The mutakallimun claim that an angel can transform its form, so how can I be certain that my son is not Jibril or that a fly is not Jibril? The objector points out that according to Islamic tradition, Jibril appeared in the form of Dihya al-Kalbi. Therefore, there is a possibility that any person or thing could be an angel in disguise, invalidating our empirical certainty. The answer is that we should reformulate the proposition: instead of saying 'this son of mine is not Jibril' (which includes a demonstrative 'this' that refers to the physical appearance), we should say 'my son, the one I consider as my son, is not Jibril'—meaning the person who has the relationship of son to me, in his current form, is not Jibril. This judgment is not based on sensory perception alone but on the relationship and the fact that the form is not that of Jibril. Therefore, the proposition is not a sensory one that would be subject to the same doubt. Thus, the objection does not undermine the distinction between necessary and customary judgments.

جبريل
دحية الكلبي
الحسيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection that if angels can appear in human form, then no empirical identification can be certain.
Correction: The answer shows that the judgment about my son is not based solely on sensory appearance but on the relationship and the fact that his current form is not that of Jibril. The reformulated proposition is not a purely sensory one, so the objection does not undermine all empirical certainty.

B) Say 'My son, the one considered my son, is not Jibril'.

Key Takeaway
The reliability of empirical judgments about individuals is preserved by understanding that the judgment is based on relationship and form, not merely sensory appearance.

Certainty Despite Possibility in Ordinary Matters
Argument defending certainty in ordinary matters against ontological possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why ordinary certainties are not undermined by the mere possibility of their opposites.

Core Idea
Ontological possibility does not contradict actual certitude.

This passage tackles a fundamental challenge: if God (or celestial motions) can bring about any event, how can we be certain of everyday facts? The author argues that the mere possibility of the opposite does not destroy certainty, as shown by our unshakable certainty in basic sense-perceptions despite their logical possibility being otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage as a whole
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a challenge presented by two groups, a specific objection about angels, and an answer.
The passage is dense; do not get stuck on details yet.
2
Identify the two reasons for doubt
The mutakallimun attribute all things to a powerful free-chooser, so improbable events are possible. The philosophers attribute terrestrial events to rare celestial configurations. Both imply that ordinary facts could be otherwise.
These are the two main sources of the possibility of error.
3
Understand the objection about Jibril
The mutakallim himself admits angels can change form. So why is he certain a fly is not Jibril? This seems to be a contradiction.
The objection targets those who accept both divine power and ordinary certainty.
4
See how sense-perception provides the answer
The author uses the example of a body occupying space: we are certain of it, even though its opposite is logically possible. This shows that possibility does not negate certainty.
The same logic applies to ordinary matters.

لفظ هذا، ويقال : أنا أجزم بأن ابني أي من حكم بكونه ابني، ووصف ببنوتي وولدي وهو على صورته وصفته الآن ليس بجبريل، لا تكون القضية من الحسيات، إذ هم قائلون بالحسيات وكون القضية منها يقتضي القدح فيها أيضاً. قوله : ( وكان له أخرى إلخ أي تارة أخرى. قوله : (دوي) أي صوت خفي كدوي الذباب فيجوز أن تكون الذبابة جبريل بتبديل الصورة ودويها دويه. قوله : (كما في المحسوسات إلخ إشارة إلى نقض تلك الشبهة فإنها جارية فيها مع أنهم قائلون بها قوله: (وكان له أخرى دوي كدوي الذباب فيه بحث لأن المستفاد من هذا النقل أن جبرئيل عليه السلام كان له دوي كدوي الذباب، وهذا لا يستلزم كونه على صورته حتى يستدل به على تجويز أهل الملة كون الذبابة التي نراها جبرئيل عليه السلام وليس الكلام في التجويز في نفس الأمر بل في الاستدلال عليه بهذا المنقول تدبر.
The Fourth Observatory - On Proving Necessary Sciences. Then, if we reflect on these propositions that we mentioned, we do not find them among those that are permissible to be certain about, so possibility (of error) exists in all of them by agreement of the rational people. As for the mutakallimun, because all things are attributed to the powerful free-chooser (al-qādir al-mukhtār), perhaps He necessitated something from that, due to possibility, for these very improbable things are certainly possible in themselves, and the generality of power (qudra) over all possibles, whether customary or improbable. As for the philosophers (ḥukamā'), because terrestrial events are attributed to celestial configurations arising from their motions, perhaps a strange celestial shape occurred that did not happen before or occurred but does not recur except in thousands of years such that histories cannot record them, so that strange shape necessitated that wondrous thing. Also, he only detailed these previous propositions because the mutakallim says that what is detrimental to them occurs, meaning the transformation of the angel's form. So I am certain that my son is not Jibril, and likewise the fly we see is not Jibril, while you, O people of the religion, permit that, since you transmitted that Jibril used to appear sometimes in the form of Diḥya al-Kalbī and had another time a buzz like the buzz of a fly. The answer: possibility does not negate certainty of occurrence, just as in some sense-perceptions, for we are certain that this body occupies this space at this moment with a certainty not penetrated by doubt, even though its opposite is possible in itself. So it became clear that certainty in ordinary matters (al-ādiyyāt) is in its proper place, and there is no possibility of the opposite that is detrimental to certainty; as for the possibility of the opposite in the sense of its intrinsic possibility, it is not detrimental, as in certain sense-perceptions. This has already been mentioned in the definition of knowledge (ʿilm). His statement: 'We do not find them among those that are permissible to be certain about, let alone to be certain' – this is sufficient. His statement: 'so possibility existed' – no need for this premise. His statement: 'by agreement of the rational people' – it is attached to 'we do not find'. His statement: 'As for the mutakallimun, because all things are attributed to the powerful free-chooser' – it is said: relying on attribution to the powerful free-chooser is not sound, because the mutakallimun say that God's custom in creating man is gradual, and He said, 'And you will never find any change in the sunna of Allah' [33:62]. It is answered: this is a transmitted proof definitive in transmission but speculative in indication, so it does not yield certainty about gradual creation, because it can be interpreted by implication: 'the estimation is that you will never find any change in the sunna of Allah unless He wants to change it by breaking His custom.' His statement: 'so that strange shape necessitated that wondrous thing' – it is by means of a specific preparedness that occurred in matter due to it. His statement: 'that my son is not Jibril' – it is said: it is appropriate to drop the word 'this' and say 'I am certain that my son' – meaning the one judged to be my son, described as my son and offspring, being now in his form and description – 'is not Jibril', so the proposition would not be a sensory proposition, since they affirm sensory propositions and the proposition being one of them would require impugnment in it also. His statement: 'and he had another time' – i.e. another time. His statement: 'buzz' – i.e. a faint sound like the buzz of a fly, so it is possible that the fly is Jibril by a transformation of the form and its buzz is his buzz. His statement: 'as in sense-perceptions' etc. is an indication of refuting that doubt, for it applies to them even though they affirm them. His statement: 'and he had another time a buzz like the buzz of a fly' – there is discussion because what is understood from this transmission is that Jibril (peace be upon him) had a buzz like the buzz of a fly, and this does not entail that he was in its form so that one could use it as evidence for the permission of the people of the religion that the fly we see is Jibril (peace be upon him). The discussion is not about permission in the actual matter, but about using this transmitted report as evidence for it. Ponder.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This is an argumentative passage defending certitude (jazm) in ordinary matters (al-ādiyyāt) despite the ontological possibility of their opposites. The author first states that the propositions under discussion are not such that absolute certainty is permissible in them, because a possibility of error exists universally. Two groups give reasons for this: The mutakallimūn attribute all things to a powerful free-chooser who could bring about extraordinary events; the philosophers attribute terrestrial events to rare celestial configurations that might produce unprecedented effects. The author then sharpens the challenge: the mutakallim himself acknowledges the possibility of angels transforming forms, so why is he certain that his son is not Jibril? The objector notes that Muslims transmit that Jibril appeared as Diḥya and had a buzz like a fly. The answer is that such possibility (imkān) does not negate actual certainty (jazm) any more than it does for sense-perceptions: we are certain that a body occupies space despite the logical possibility that it does not. The commentary then clarifies each phrase. It addresses an objection about relying on transmitted texts (like the verse on sunna) that are definitive in transmission but speculative in interpretation, so they do not yield certainty against the possibility of breaking custom. It also discusses the wording of the certainty about the son and fly, noting that the proposition should avoid being sensory to escape the doubt that sensory propositions themselves could be doubted. Finally, it examines the transmission about Jibril's buzz, concluding that it does not prove that a fly we see could be Jibril in his actual form.

Key Terms
العاديات
الجزم
الإمكان
القادر المختار
تبديل صورة الملك
المحسوسات
اليقينية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that any possibility of error destroys all certainty.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only a real (not merely ontological) possibility undermines certitude; intrinsic possibility does not.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the possibility that Jibril could appear in the form of a fly not negate the certainty that the fly we see is not Jibril?
A) Because Jibril never actually appears in fly form.
B) Because ontological possibility does not contradict actual certitude, as in sense-perceptions.
C) Because the report about Jibril's buzz is unreliable.
D) Because the mutakallimun reject such possibilities.

Key Takeaway
Ordinary certainties remain valid because mere ontological possibility does not undermine actual certitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What ordinary certainty do you hold most strongly in your daily life? Could you still act upon it if you truly entertained the possibility that it might not be as it seems? What does this lesson demand of your heart regarding that certainty?
?
You recite the shahada with certainty, but do you ever allow the ontological possibility of error to creep into your heart and weaken your conviction? How does this lesson's distinction between possibility and actual certainty challenge that doubt?
?
Imagine you encountered someone who claims that because angels can appear as humans, they cannot be certain of anyone's identity. How would you use the lesson's argument to not only refute them but also strengthen your own trust in the ordinary signs of people's identities? What does that reveal about your reliance on Allah's custom?

The Third Doubt: Temperament and Habits Influence Beliefs
Presents the third doubt with two concrete examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third doubt against self-evident truths, which claims that temperament and habits influence beliefs and thereby challenge the certainty of self-evident knowledge.

Core Idea
Our beliefs can be shaped by temperament and custom, not just pure reason.

This lesson introduces the third doubt raised by skeptics against self-evident truths. The doubt argues that our physical temperament and cultural habits significantly affect our beliefs. For instance, a person with a strong constitution may approve of causing pain, while a weak-hearted person abhors it, leading to different moral judgments. Similarly, growing up in a particular school of thought can make one certain of its correctness, even if it is false. This suggests that what we consider self-evident may actually be influenced by universal temperament or custom, thus undermining its certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt
Read the opening statement: the doubt is that temperaments and habits have an effect on beliefs.
This is directed against those who deny self-evident truths.
2
Examine the first example
Consider the example of strong vs. weak heart regarding causing pain. How does temperament affect moral judgment?
The strong heart approves of causing pain, the weak disapproves.
3
Examine the second example
Look at the example of habituation to a madhhab. Someone raised in a tradition becomes certain of it through mere repetition, even if false.
4
Understand the conclusion
The doubt concludes that even self-evident judgments may be due to universal temperament or custom, so they are not certain.
This challenges the reliability of self-evident truths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة لمنكري البديهيات فقط أن يقال: للأمزجة والعادات تأثير في الاعتقادات فقوى القلب بحسب المزاج يستحسن الإيلام ولا يستقبحه بل ربما يلتذ به وضعيف القلب يستقبحه جداً، ولذلك ترى بعضهم لا يجوزون ذبح الحيوانات للانتفاع بأكلها ومن مارس مذهباً من المذاهب حقاً كان أو باطلاً، واعتاد به برهة من الزمان ونشأ عليه فإنه بمجرد اعتياده به من غير أن يلوح له ما يظهر به حقيته يجزم بصحته، وإن كان باطلاً وبطلان ما يخالفه وإن كان حقاً فجاز أن يكون الجزم من بديهة العقل في الكل أي كل ما حكمت به لمزاج أو عادة عامين لجميع أفراد الإنسان المتفقين في البديهيات، فلا تكون يقينية كالقضايا الصادرة من الأمزجة والعادات المخصوصة لا يقال : نحن نفرض أنفسنا خالية عن جميع الأمزجة والعادات ومع ذلك نجد من أنفسنا الجزم بهذه الأمور البديهية، فالحاكم فيها صريح العقل بلا تأثير من مزاج أو عادة لأنا نقول : لا نسلم إمكان فرض الخلو عن قوله: (لأنا نقول : لا نسلم إمكان فرض الخلو إذ قد لا نشعر ببعض إلخ) قيل : عليه إمكان فرض الخلو إنما يستدعي إمكان الشعور لا الشعور بالفعل، فالدليل لا يطابق الدعوى، وأجيب
The third doubt for those who deny self-evident truths (badīhiyyāt) only is that it is said: Temperaments (amzija) and habits (ʿādāt) have an effect on beliefs. So a strong heart, due to temperament, approves of causing pain and does not disapprove of it, but rather perhaps takes pleasure in it, while a weak heart disapproves of it greatly. That is why you see some people do not permit slaughtering animals for benefit from eating them. And whoever practices a school of thought (madhhab), whether true or false, and becomes accustomed to it for a period of time and grows up on it, then merely by his habituation to it without something appearing to him that manifests its truth, he is certain of its correctness even if it is false, and of the falsehood of what opposes it even if it is true. So it is permissible that the certainty from the self-evident intellect (badīhat al-ʿaql) in all of it, i.e., all that it judges, is due to a temperament or a habit that is universal for all individuals of mankind who agree on self-evident truths; so these are not certain like the propositions that issue from specific temperaments and habits. One should not say: We assume ourselves free from all temperaments and habits, and yet we find in ourselves certainty about these self-evident matters, so the judge in them is pure intellect without influence of temperament or habit. For we say: We do not grant the possibility of assuming freedom from, – regarding his statement: 'For we say: We do not grant the possibility of assuming freedom from, since we may not be aware of some [influence], etc.' – it is said: the possibility of assuming freedom requires only the possibility of awareness, not actual awareness, so the evidence does not match the claim, and it is answered.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents the third doubt (shubha) against those who deny self-evident truths (badīhiyyāt). The doubt argues that temperaments and habits influence beliefs: for example, a strong temperament may find pleasure in causing pain, while a weak one abhors it, leading some to prohibit animal slaughter. Similarly, someone raised in a particular madhhab becomes certain of its truth through habituation alone, even if it is false. Therefore, the certainty we attribute to self-evident intellect might itself be due to universal temperament or custom, making it unreliable. The response begins by rejecting this: we can assume ourselves free from all temperaments and habits and still find certainty in self-evident matters, showing that pure intellect is the judge. The objector then counters that it is impossible to assume such freedom because we may be unaware of hidden influences. The final part notes that the possibility of assuming freedom only requires the possibility of awareness, not actual awareness, so the objection fails. The passage thus defends the reliability of self-evident truths against psychological and sociological reductionism.

Key Terms
الشبهة الثالثة
الأمزجة
العادات
البديهيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-evident truths are absolutely certain and cannot be influenced by anything.
Correction: The doubt argues that even self-evident truths might be influenced by universal temperament or habit, challenging their absolute certainty.

Checkpoint
What is the main claim of the third doubt?
A) Self-evident truths are always certain regardless of temperament.
B) Temperaments and habits have an effect on beliefs, potentially undermining the certainty of self-evident truths.
C) Only weak hearts are influenced by temperament.
D) Habituation to a madhhab always leads to true beliefs.

Key Takeaway
The third doubt challenges the certainty of self-evident truths by arguing that temperament and habits can influence our beliefs, as illustrated by examples from moral judgment and doctrinal habituation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold that you have never seriously questioned. Is it possible that this belief is merely a product of your temperament or the habits of your environment? If that is the case, what would it take for you to genuinely examine whether it is true in itself?
?
The passage mentions strong-hearted people who find pleasure in causing pain. Are there moral intuitions you have that might be colored by your own emotional makeup rather than objective truth? How would you distinguish between a feeling and a truth?
?
If the third doubt were valid, your certainty in the oneness of Allah could be the result of a universal habit rather than rational proof. Does that possibility disturb you? What can you do to ground your faith beyond mere custom?

Defending Self-Evident Truths: The Response to the Doubt
Presents the response, objection, and rebuttal regarding the third doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the response to the third doubt, which argues that we can abstract from temperament and habits and still find certainty in self-evident truths.

Core Idea
We can set aside temperament and habits and still know self-evident truths with certainty.

This lesson presents the counterargument to the third doubt. The responder insists that we can assume ourselves free from all temperamental and habitual influences and still find within ourselves certainty about self-evident matters, proving that pure intellect is the judge. The objector then challenges the possibility of such an assumption, and the responder replies that the possibility of assuming freedom only requires the possibility of awareness, not actual awareness. This defense upholds the reliability of self-evident knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the counterargument
Read the response: 'We assume ourselves free from all temperaments and habits and still find certainty in self-evident matters.' This is the key claim.
The responder asserts that pure intellect judges without external influence.
2
Consider the objection
The objector replies that it is impossible to assume such freedom because we may be unaware of hidden influences.
This challenges the possibility of abstraction.
3
Analyze the rebuttal
The responder answers that the possibility of assuming freedom only requires the possibility of awareness, not actual awareness, so the objection fails.
This defends the counterargument.
4
Reflect on the outcome
The doubt is rebutted, and self-evident truths remain secure as certain knowledge derived from pure intellect.

The third doubt for those who deny self-evident truths (badīhiyyāt) only is that it is said: Temperaments (amzija) and habits (ʿādāt) have an effect on beliefs. So a strong heart, due to temperament, approves of causing pain and does not disapprove of it, but rather perhaps takes pleasure in it, while a weak heart disapproves of it greatly. That is why you see some people do not permit slaughtering animals for benefit from eating them. And whoever practices a school of thought (madhhab), whether true or false, and becomes accustomed to it for a period of time and grows up on it, then merely by his habituation to it without something appearing to him that manifests its truth, he is certain of its correctness even if it is false, and of the falsehood of what opposes it even if it is true. So it is permissible that the certainty from the self-evident intellect (badīhat al-ʿaql) in all of it, i.e., all that it judges, is due to a temperament or a habit that is universal for all individuals of mankind who agree on self-evident truths; so these are not certain like the propositions that issue from specific temperaments and habits. One should not say: We assume ourselves free from all temperaments and habits, and yet we find in ourselves certainty about these self-evident matters, so the judge in them is pure intellect without influence of temperament or habit. For we say: We do not grant the possibility of assuming freedom from, – regarding his statement: 'For we say: We do not grant the possibility of assuming freedom from, since we may not be aware of some [influence], etc.' – it is said: the possibility of assuming freedom requires only the possibility of awareness, not actual awareness, so the evidence does not match the claim, and it is answered.

Commentary
This passage presents the third doubt (shubha) against those who deny self-evident truths (badīhiyyāt). How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your certainty about the existence and oneness of Allah, do you allow the thought 'maybe I'm just influenced by my upbringing' to weaken your conviction? How would living by the principle that possible awareness is enough change your daily reliance on that certainty?

The Third Doubt: Influence of Temperaments and Habits
Al-Iji presents the third doubt of deniers of self-evident truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third doubt raised by deniers of self-evident truths, which argues that temperaments and habits influence beliefs and potentially undermine the certainty of self-evident judgments.

Core Idea
Temperaments and habits can make false beliefs seem certain.

Al-Iji presents a powerful challenge: if our temperaments and habits can make us certain of falsehoods, how can we trust our self-evident beliefs? This lesson unpacks the denier's claim and its examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt statement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the denier presents the claim that temperaments and habits influence beliefs.
The denier uses examples: a strong heart may not find cruelty bad, and people become certain of false doctrines through habituation.
2
Identify the conclusion
Find the conclusion of the doubt: that the certainty of self-evident truths might not be certain, just like propositions arising from specific temperaments and habits.
The doubt ends with a direct challenge to the universal agreement on self-evident truths.
3
Reflect on the challenge
Consider why this doubt is considered a serious objection to the reliability of self-evident truths. Think about how it could be answered.
The next lesson will present Al-Iji's refutation.
